(فتح) في الفصلين إلخ أي في فصل الصلح عن إقرار والصلح عن إنكار وسكوت، وقد مر حكمهما، فإن كان عن إقرار رجع بعد الهلاك إلى المدعي وإن كان عن إنكار رجع بالدعوى كذا في "الدرر"، واقتصر على ذكر الإنكار ولم يذكر السكوت؛ لأنه في حكمه رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٢/٢] (فتح) فصل: لما فرغ من بيان الصلح وشرائطه وأنواعه، شرع في بيان ما يجوز الصلح عنه وما لا يجوز.(حموي، فتح) الصلح جائز إلخ لأنه في معنى البيع في حقهما إن وقع بمال عن إقرار، أو في حق المدعي وحده إن وقع عن إنكار أو سكوت، وفي حق الآخر لافتداء اليمين وقطع الخصومة رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٢/٢] (فتح)
And it becomes obligatory if he made a compromise on a house with both (denial and silence). And if the disputed property is claimed by a third party, the plaintiff returns to litigation, i.e., pre-emption for the neighbor with denial or silence, i.e., all of it after the compromise, and the consideration. If some of it, then proportionally. And if the subject of the compromise is claimed, or part of it, = it is because he claims that it, i.e., the claim, i.e., all the consideration, his house, and that he retains it as it was, and that what he paid to the plaintiff is not compensation for it, but only to redeem the oath and end the litigation. But the pre-emptor may take the plaintiff's place. If the plaintiff has evidence for the house, the pre-emptor presents it to prove that the house belongs to the plaintiff, and takes the house by pre-emption, because presenting evidence shows that the compromise was in the nature of a sale. Similarly, if he has no evidence, the plaintiff takes an oath and then refuses. ('Ayni, Fath with addition) 'And it becomes obligatory if he made a compromise etc.': i.e., pre-emption is obligatory for the neighbor against the plaintiff if they, i.e., the plaintiff and defendant, made a compromise on a house with both, i.e., with denial and silence. Its form: a man claimed something from another, and he compromised with him on a house and gave it to him; pre-emption becomes obligatory in it because he takes it as compensation for what he paid, so it is an exchange in his claim, so pre-emption is obligatory in it. Even if he claimed a house and the other denied, and he compromised with him on another house, pre-emption is obligatory in the house on which the compromise was made, not the other, as mentioned. The other's denial of exchange does not prevent the obligation of pre-emption in it. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/231) And he said in al-Fath: because the plaintiff takes it as compensation for his right in his claim, and the other's denial of exchange does not prevent the obligation of pre-emption in it. Because the plaintiff claims the rightful owner. 'And if the disputed is claimed etc.': i.e., if the disputed property is claimed by a third party: a man claimed something from a person, and he denied, then he compromised with him on something, then the claimed property, i.e., the disputed property, is claimed by a third party with possession and increase. The plaintiff returns, i.e., if the disputed is claimed, the plaintiff (as agent) returns to litigation with the claimant, meaning he litigates the claimant as he had litigated the one with whom he compromised by paying something, and he returns the consideration he took from the denier. (Habib) (al-Rahman: 'and he returns the consideration': i.e., the compensation he took from the defendant's property. Explanation: Zayd claimed a house in 'Amr's hand. 'Amr denied or remained silent, then he compromised on one hundred. The hundred is in Zayd's hand, the house in 'Amr's hand. Then all the house is claimed. So Zayd returns the hundred to 'Amr, and returns to litigation over the house with the claimant because he stands in place of the defendant when the plaintiff took from him, so he may litigate him. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/231] 'And if some of it etc.': [i.e., he returns to litigation with the claimant proportionally and returns the consideration to the defendant proportionally] i.e., if part, meaning part of the disputed property, is claimed, then he returns to litigation and the plaintiff returns to the defendant proportionally to what was claimed. Its form: half the house is claimed by a claimant in the mentioned case; Zayd returns fifty to 'Amr and returns to the claimant for litigation over the half that was claimed. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/231) 'The subject of the compromise etc.': i.e., that on which the compromise was made, either all of it or part of it is claimed, the plaintiff returns to the claim for all of it if all the consideration is claimed, or he returns to the claim for part of it if part of the consideration is claimed; because the plaintiff did not abandon the claim unless the consideration was delivered to him. If it is not delivered, he returns to the substitute, which is the claim. Unlike if the compromise was made using words of sale, where one says 'I sold you this thing for this' and the other says 'I bought,' in which case the plaintiff at the time of claim returns against the defendant for the claimed property itself, not the claim; because the defendant's action of sale indicates his acknowledgment that the claimed property is the plaintiff's property, so his denial is not considered. In contrast to compromise, because nothing indicates his acknowledgment of ownership; compromise may occur to end litigation. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/231] 'He returns to the claim etc.': i.e., if the subject of the compromise, which is the consideration, is claimed, the plaintiff returns to the previous claim, and the compromise is void. He said in al-Bahr: except if it is something not determinate by specification, and it is of the same type as the claimed property; then he returns with an equivalent to what was claimed, and the compromise is not void. As if he claimed one thousand, and compromised on one hundred and took possession, then he returns to him with one hundred when it is claimed, whether the compromise was after acknowledgment or before, as if he found it to be counterfeit or defective. In contrast to if it is of a different type, like dinars, if they are claimed after separation, the compromise is void; if before separation, he returns with an equivalent, and the compromise is not void, like copper coins. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7/367] 'In all of it' i.e., in all the disputed property if all the consideration is claimed. (M) 'And destruction of the consideration etc.' [all or part after specification] i.e., if the consideration is destroyed before delivery, its ruling is like its being claimed, i.e., the consideration, in the two cases. If like its being claimed, the compromise is void; because destruction of the consideration in a sale voids the sale, so too here. If part is destroyed, it is like part being claimed, so the compromise is void to that extent and remains for the rest, as in the case of claim. This if the consideration is determinate by specification. If it is not determinate by specification, like dirhams and dinars, it is not void by destruction. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/232] Because they are not determinate in contracts and dissolutions, so the contract does not attach to them when indicated, but rather attaches to their equivalents in the dhimma, so destruction is not conceivable. (Fath) 'In the two cases etc.': i.e., in the case of compromise after acknowledgment and after denial or silence. Their ruling has passed. If after acknowledgment, after destruction he returns to the plaintiff; if after denial, he returns to the claim. So in al-Durar. He limited it to denial and did not mention silence because it is similar in ruling. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/232] (Fath) Section: When he finished explaining compromise, its conditions and types, he began explaining what is permissible to compromise on and what is not. (Hamawi, Fath) 'Compromise is permissible' etc. because it is in the meaning of sale regarding both parties if it occurs with property after acknowledgment, or regarding the plaintiff alone if it occurs after denial or silence, and regarding the other to redeem the oath and end litigation. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/232] (Fath)
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses several key rulings on compromise (sulh) in property disputes. It opens with a ruling on pre-emption (shuf'a): if a plaintiff and defendant compromise on a house (with the defendant denying or remaining silent), the neighbor has a right of pre-emption over that house. The reasoning is that the plaintiff, in his own view, takes the house as compensation for his claim, making it an exchange. Even if the defendant denies it is an exchange, the neighbor's right remains. The commentator then addresses cases where a third party claims ownership of the disputed property (istihqaq). If the entire disputed property is claimed, the plaintiff returns to litigation with the claimant and returns any consideration he took from the defendant. If only part is claimed, the return is proportional. Next, if the consideration itself (the thing given by the defendant) is claimed by a third party, the plaintiff returns to his original claim (i.e., the compromise is void). An exception is made if the consideration is of the same type as the claimed property and not specifically determined; in that case, the plaintiff demands an equivalent, and the compromise remains valid. Similarly, if the consideration is destroyed before delivery, the same rules apply as in the claim case. The passage concludes with a general principle: compromise (sulh) is permissible in all property claims because it is analogous to a sale — in the case of acknowledgment, it is a sale for both parties; in denial or silence, it is a sale for the plaintiff and a way to end litigation for the defendant. The commentator notes that compromise differs from an express sale because it does not imply acknowledgment of ownership, so if the consideration is claimed, the plaintiff can revert to the original claim.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A contract to end a dispute by mutual agreement, often involving payment of a consideration.
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of a neighbor or co-owner to purchase property that has been sold or transferred through an exchange contract.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A third party's claim of ownership over property that is the subject of a contract.
المعاوضة (al-mu'awada): A contract of exchange where each party gives something in return for another.
البدل (al-badal): The consideration given in exchange, often the property transferred in compromise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi ruling, if a third party claims ownership of the consideration (badal) given in a compromise, what is the default effect on the compromise?
A) The compromise is void, and the plaintiff returns to his original claim.
B) The plaintiff keeps the consideration and litigates the claimant.
C) The compromise remains valid, but the plaintiff pays the claimant.
D) The defendant must replace the consideration.

Key Takeaway
Compromise in property disputes is treated like a sale for pre-emption, and third-party claims or destruction of the consideration can void or adjust the compromise, except when the consideration is indeterminate (like money).

Sulh in Slavery Claims: Emancipation without Patronage
Expansion on sulh in slavery claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh in a claim of slavery and explain why wala' is not established.

Core Idea
Sulh in a slavery claim frees the defendant without giving the claimant wala'.

This lesson examines a unique application of sulh (settlement) in the context of claims of slavery. How does the law harmonize the parties' interests while upholding the principle that freedom is not bargained away lightly? The passage clarifies the effect and limits of such a settlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base claim and parties
One party (claimant) claims that the other (defendant) is his slave. The other denies this and claims to be free. They reach a settlement (sulh) where the defendant pays something to the claimant.
This is a dispute over status, not a sale.
2
Determine the effect for each party
For the claimant, the sulh is treated as emancipation (itq) for a consideration (mal). For the defendant, it ends the dispute and confirms his freedom.
The sulh is valid despite the uncertainly because it can be construed favorably for both.
3
Note the absence of wala'
The claimant does not acquire wala' (patronage) over the defendant because the defendant denies being a slave. Wala' only arises when the emancipator acknowledges the slavery and frees the slave.
Wala' gives inheritance rights; here it would be unjust to impose on the defendant.
4
Understand the special evidence rule
If later the claimant produces evidence that the defendant was indeed a slave, it is accepted only for establishing wala', not to revert the defendant to slavery. The sulh has already made him free.
This prevents re-enslavement while allowing possible wala' if proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرق إلخ: أي الصلح جائز أيضاً في دعوى الرق، وكان الصلح في حق المدعي عتقاً على مال، وفي حق الآخر لدفع الخصومة؛ لأنه أمكن تصحيحه بهذا الاعتبار، فجاز إلا أنه لا ولاء له عليه؛ لأنه ينكر العتق، ويدعي أنه حرّ الأصل إلا أن يقيم المدعي البينة بعد ذلك، فتقبل بينته في حق ثبوت الولاء عليه لا غير، حتى لا يكون رقيقاً؛ لأنه معتقاً بالصلح، فلا يعود رقيقاً. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٣/٢]
‘And slavery etc.’: meaning sulh is also permissible in a claim of slavery. The sulh is, in the right of the claimant, emancipation for a consideration, and in the right of the other, for ending the dispute. Because it is possible to validate it by this consideration, so it is permissible. However, he does not have wala' over him, because he denies emancipation and claims he is originally free, unless after that the claimant produces evidence, then his evidence is accepted only for the purpose of establishing wala' over him, not otherwise, so that he does not become a slave; because he is emancipated by the sulh, so he does not return to slavery.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This is a ruling statement. Sulh is permissible in a claim of slavery. For the claimant (who claims the defendant is his slave), the sulh counts as emancipation for a price. For the defendant, it ends the dispute. However, the claimant does not gain the right of wala' (patronage) because the defendant denies being a slave. If later the claimant produces evidence of slavery, it is accepted only for establishing wala', not to revert the person to slavery, since he is already free by the sulh.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala'): The bond of patronage that arises from emancipating a slave, giving the patron inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the effect of sulh in a claim of slavery according to the Hanafi school?
A) The defendant becomes a slave of the claimant
B) The defendant is emancipated and the claimant gains wala'
C) The defendant is emancipated without wala'
D) The sulh is invalid

Key Takeaway
Sulh in a slavery claim emancipates the defendant for a consideration, but the claimant does not gain wala' due to the denial of slavery.

Sulh of a Ma'dhun Slave for His Own Murder
Ruling on ma'dhun slave's sulh for intentional murder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a ma'dhun slave's sulh for intentional murder, explain its binding effect on the heir and master, and distinguish it from the case of a mukatab.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun slave's sulh for his own life is valid against the heir but deferred in payment and not binding on the master.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the nuanced position of a ma'dhun slave who commits intentional murder and negotiates a settlement (sulh) with the victim's heir. Unlike a free person or a mukatab, a ma'dhun slave's authority is limited to trade, so his settlement for his own person raises special issues regarding enforceability and payment timing. We will unpack the ruling and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The case involves a ma'dhun slave (a slave given permission to trade) who intentionally kills a free man. The heir of the deceased has the right to either demand qisas (retaliation) or accept sulh (blood money).
Ma'dhun slave's permission is only for trade, not for personal acts like murder settlements.
2
Determine the validity of the sulh
The text states the sulh is 'not valid' regarding its effect on the master, but 'valid between him and the heir.' This means the slave's own agreement is legally effective in sparing his life, but it does not obligate the master to pay compensation.
The heir cannot claim qisas after accepting sulh, even if payment is not immediate.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The slave is mukallaf (legally responsible), so his acts in his own right are valid. However, his master's permission only covers trade, so the sulh cannot bind the master. This balances the slave's personal responsibility with his limited capacity to incur debts for his master.
The ruling uses istihsan (juristic preference) to defer payment.
4
Understand the payment deferment
Compensation is not due immediately because the slave lacks personal wealth. It is deferred until after his emancipation (e.g., by manumission). This is a concession to practicality, avoiding immediate execution or burdening the master.
The deferment is a key difference from a free man's immediate liability.
5
Contrast with the mukatab
A mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) can settle on his own behalf because he is like a free man—his master's control is minimal, and he may acquire wealth. This contrast highlights the ma'dhun's restricted authority.
Mukatab's sulh is immediately binding and payment is due at the agreed time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتل العبد إلخ: أي وإن قتل العبد المأذون رجلاً عمداً لم يجز صلحه عن نفسه؛ لأنه لا يجوز له أن يتصرف إلا فيما هو من باب التجارة وتصرفه في نفسه ليس من باب التجارة، فلا ينفذ في حق المولى إذا كان الصلح بعوض، ويصح بينه وبين ولي المقتول، حتى لا يجوز له قتله بعد العفو؛ لأنه مكلف، فيصح تصرفه في حق نفسه، ولا يجب عليه بدل الصلح في الحال، ويتأخر إلى ما بعد العتق، بخلاف المكاتب حيث يجوز له أن يصالح عن نفسه؛ لأنه كالحر بخروجه عن يد المولى رمز الحقائق: ۲۳۳/۲]
‘And if the slave kills etc.’: meaning if a ma'dhun slave kills a man intentionally, his sulh on his own behalf is not valid, because he is not allowed to act except in matters of trade, and acting in his own person is not trade, so it is not effective against the master if the sulh involves compensation. However, it is valid between him and the heir of the deceased, such that it is not permissible to kill him after the pardon, because he is legally responsible, so his action is valid regarding his own person. But the compensation for sulh is not due immediately, and is deferred until after his emancipation. As opposed to a mukatab, who may settle on his own behalf, because he is like a free man in leaving the master's control.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the nature of the ma'dhun slave's authority. A ma'dhun slave is permitted to conduct trade, but settling for his own life for intentional murder is not related to trade, so the sulh is not binding on the master. However, the sulh is valid between the slave and the victim's heir, meaning the heir cannot retaliate after accepting the sulh. The payment is delayed until the slave becomes free. A mukatab (who is purchasing his freedom) is different: he can settle on his own behalf because he is like a free man.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately
B) After the master pays
C) After the slave becomes free
D) It is never due

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave's sulh for intentional murder is valid against the heir and defers payment to after freedom, but does not bind the master.

Sulh for Excess Over Fixed Value in Joint Emancipation
Ruling on sulh for excess in joint emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh for more than half the slave's value in joint emancipation and explain the reasoning based on the shariah-fixed value and qiyas.

Core Idea
Sulh for more than the shariah-fixed value is invalid because the sacred estimation overrides private agreement.

In joint emancipation, a partner's share is valued by shariah. If the emancipator settles with the partner for more than half the slave's value, the excess is invalid by consensus among the three Hanafi imams. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including Abu Hanifa's proof from the hadith-based valuation and the qiyas from the usurper case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the emancipator's settlement with the partner for more than half the slave's value. The excess is invalid (batil) by consensus.
The half-value portion is valid; only the extra is void.
2
Understand the consensus among the three imams
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn all agree on the invalidity, though their reasoning differs slightly.
For Sahibayn it is apparent; for Abu Hanifa it follows from the shariah-fixed value.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof: shariah-fixed value
The slave's value in emancipation is specified by text (hadith); this is akin to a judge's determination. Paying more is like riba.
The shariah's estimation is above a judge's estimation.
4
Apply the qiyas from the usurper case
If a usurper settles after judgment for more than the value, it is invalid. Here, the shariah-fixed value is even more authoritative, so the ruling applies a fortiori.
The reasoning is 'first awla' (even more so).
5
Note the exception: settlement with goods
If the settlement is made with a commodity (not cash), it is valid regardless of value because the excess is not apparent when types differ.
This exception confirms the general rule that cash excess is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا : أي لا يصح الصلح في حق الزيادة من نصف قيمة العبد بالاتفاق؛ أما عندهما فظاهر، وأما عند أبي حنيفة فلأن القيمة في العتق منصوص عليها، وتقدير الشرع لا يكون دون تقدير القاضي، فلا تجوز الزيادة عليه، فإذا لم يجز صلح الغاصب عن المغصوب المتلف بما زاد على قيمته بعد القضاء فلا يجوز الصلح في هذه الصورة على أكثر من نصف قيمة العبد بالطريق الأولى؛ لأن تقدير الشرع فوق تقدير القاضي، بخلاف ما تقدم من الصورة الخلافية؛ لأنها غير منصوص عليها، وإن صالحه على عرض جاز كيفما كان؛ لما بينا أنه لا يظهر الفضل عند اختلاف الجنس. [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٤/٢] وإنما قيد بقوله: "موسراً"؛ لأنه إذا كان معسراً تجب سعاية النصف على العبد. (فتح، ملا مسكين بتضوح من المحشي)
‘No’: i.e., the sulh for the excess over half the value of the slave is not valid by consensus. As for them (Sahibayn), it is apparent; as for Abu Hanifa, because the value in emancipation is specified by text, and the shariah's estimation is not less than the judge's estimation, so excess over it is not permissible. So if the usurper's sulh for destroyed property with excess after judgment is not valid, then sulh in this case for more than half the value of the slave is even more so, because the shariah's estimation is above the judge's estimation. Unlike the earlier disputed case, because it was not specified by text. If he settles with him on a commodity, it is valid in any case, as we stated that excess does not appear when types differ. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 234/2] The restriction to ‘wealthy’ is because if he is insolvent, the half must be earned by the slave's work. (Fath, Mulla Miskeen with clarification from the commentator)
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This is a ruling statement with reasoning. If a wealthy person emancipates a jointly-owned slave (his share and a partner's), and then settles with the partner for more than half the slave's value, the sulh for the excess is invalid by consensus among Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. The reason: the slave's value in emancipation is specified by shariah (as per hadith), which is like a judge's determination. Paying more is like riba. This is even stronger than the previous case of usurper after judgment. However, if the settlement is in goods (not cash), it is valid because the excess is not apparent. The condition 'wealthy' is because if the emancipator is poor, the slave must work to pay half his value.

Key Terms
السعاية (al-sa'ayah): Earning by the slave to pay off his value when the master is insolvent.
الصلح (al-sulh): Settlement or compromise contract to resolve a dispute or claim.
القيمة المنصوص عليها (al-qīmah al-manṣūṣ ʿalayhā): The value fixed by shariah (e.g., through hadith) for a slave in emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the value is fixed by shariah and excess is like riba
C) Because the slave must consent
D) Because it is not a valid contract

Key Takeaway
Sulh for excess over the shariah-fixed value is invalid; the sacred estimation cannot be overridden by private agreement.

Settlement by Usurper for More Than Value
Intra-Hanafi dispute on settlement for excess over value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on settlement by a usurper for destroyed property for more than its value, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the ruling to scenarios before and after judgment.

Core Idea
Before judgment, the usurper's settlement for more than value is valid because the owner's right remains in the destroyed property.

This passage addresses a specific dispute within the Hanafi school: can a usurper settle with the owner for an amount exceeding the property's value after it has been destroyed? The answer depends on timing and the medium of settlement. Abu Hanifa permits it before judgment; the Sahibayn restrict it. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the concept that the owner's right in the property itself persists until judicial valuation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? Settlement by usurper for destroyed property for more than its value. State the basic ruling according to the text.
The act is a ṣulḥ (compromise) between usurper and owner.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Abu Hanifa holds that such settlement is valid before judgment. Why? Because the owner's right in the property itself remains until judgment; only then does it transfer to value.
3
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that it is not allowed if the excess is more than what people commonly overlook. Their proof: the obligatory is the value, and any increase is riba.
4
Analyse the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: 'his right in the destroyed property remains, and it only transfers to value by judgment, so agreement on more is a substitute, not riba.' Sahibayn's proof: 'the obligatory is the value, which is determined in currency, so the increase would be riba.'
Note that after judgment, all agree it is invalid because the judge's estimation is like the lawgiver.
5
Determine the practical difference
Before judgment: Abu Hanifa allows, Sahibayn forbid (unless trivial excess). After judgment: all forbid. For settlement with a commodity (different type), all allow because the excess is not apparent when type differs.
Also, after a valid settlement, the usurper cannot introduce evidence that the value was lower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتلف: بأن غصب عبداً مثلاً فهلك عنده ثم صالح مولاه. (عيني) بما زاد إلخ: يعني قبل القضاء بالقيمة، حتى لو كان بعد القضاء بالقيمة لا يجوز؛ لأن تقدير القاضي كالشارع، كذا في "التنوير" و"شرحه"، وإذا لم يجز الصلح على ما يزيد على القيمة بعد القضاء بها، يلزمه رد الزيادة. (فتح) على قيمته أي قيمة المغصوب بأن كان العبد يساوي خمسين فصالحه على مائة. (عيني) صح إلخ: أي ولو صالح الغاصب عن المغصوب المتلف بأن غصب عبداً مثلاً، فهلك عنده، ثم صالح مولاه بما زاد على قيمته أي قيمة المغصوب بأن كان العبد يساوي خمسين فصالحه على مائة أو صالحه بأكثر من قيمته على عرض صح الصلح عند أبي حنيفة محله، وقالا : لا يجوز على الأكثر من قيمته بما لا يتغابن الناس في مثله؛ لأن الواجب هو القيمة وهي مقدرة من النقود، فالزيادة عليها يكون ربا، وله أن حقه في الهالك باق، وإنما ينتقل إلى القيمة بالقضاء، فإذا تراضيا على الأكثر كان اعتياضاً، فلا يكون رباً، وأما الصلح بأكثر من قيمته على العرض، فهو جائز بالإجماع؛ لأن الزيادة لا تظهر عند اختلاف الجنس. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٣/٢] صح: فلا تقبل بينة الغاصب بعد الصلح أن قيمته أقل مما صالح عليه ولا رجوع للغاصب على المغصوب منه بشيء لو تصادقا بعد الصلح أنها أقل كذا في "التنوير" و"شرحه". (فتح)
The destroyed property: e.g., he usurped a slave and it perished with him, then he settled with his owner. ('Ayni) 'with what exceeds' etc.: meaning before judgment of value, so much so that if it is after judgment of value, it is not allowed; because the judge's estimation is like the lawgiver, as in al-Tanwir and its commentary. And if settlement for more than the value is not allowed after judgment, he must return the excess. (Fath) 'on its value' i.e., the value of the usurped item: for example, the slave was worth fifty and he settled for one hundred. ('Ayni) 'valid' etc.: i.e., if the usurper settles for the destroyed usurped property — e.g., he usurped a slave and it perished, then he settled with his owner for more than its value, like the slave worth fifty and he settled for one hundred, or settled with more than its value for a commodity — the settlement is valid according to Abu Hanifa, and the two said: it is not allowed for more than its value by an amount that people do not usually overlook; because the obligatory is the value, which is determined in currency, so the increase would be riba. And he (Abu Hanifa) says that his right in the destroyed property remains, and it only transfers to the value by judgment, so if they mutually agree on more, it is a substitute, so it is not riba. As for settlement for more than its value with a commodity, it is permissible by consensus because the increase does not appear when the type differs. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 233/2] 'Valid': So the witness of the usurper after settlement that its value is less than what he settled for is not accepted, nor can the usurper claim anything from the owner if they both later agree it was less, as in al-Tanwir and its commentary. (Fath)
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a usurper (ghasib) who destroys the usurped property and then settles with the owner for more than its value. According to Abu Hanifa, this settlement is valid before a judge has determined the value, because the owner's right is in the actual property (which is destroyed) and only transfers to the value by judicial decree. So agreeing on more is a mutual exchange, not riba. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is not allowed if the excess is significant and not commonly overlooked, because the obligation is the value and excess would be prohibited riba. However, if the settlement is for a commodity (different genus) for more than the value, it is unanimously valid because the excess is not apparent when types differ. Also, after the settlement, the usurper cannot introduce evidence that the value was less.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghasib): A person who takes another's property without right, a usurper.
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; prohibited excess in exchange of same genus.
قيمة (qīmah): Value; monetary compensation for destroyed property.
صلح (ṣulḥ): Settlement; compromise agreement between parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the owner has already lost the property.
B) Because the right remains in the destroyed property and transfers only by judgment.
C) Because the usurper has admitted liability.
D) Because the value is not known until judgment.

Key Takeaway
Before judicial valuation, a usurper's settlement for more than the property's value is valid according to Abu Hanifa because the owner's right remains in the property.

Settlement by Partner in Manumission Case
Ruling on settlement by partner for more than half value after manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why settlement for more than half the value is invalid and how Abu Hanifa's a fortiori reasoning (qiyas awla) differs from the Sahibayn's reasoning.

Core Idea
Fixed values cannot be exceeded: the law's estimation is above any judge's, so settlement for more is void.

When a jointly owned slave is freed by one partner, the freeing partner must compensate the other for their half. If he settles for more than that half's value, is that extra amount valid? The Hanafi school unanimously says no – but the reasons behind this ruling reveal a fascinating difference in legal reasoning. This passage presents Abu Hanifa's a fortiori argument from a related case and contrasts it with the Sahibayn's direct approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Determine the exact hukm from the passage: settlement for more than half the slave's value is invalid (la yasihh al-sulh fi haqq al-ziyada).
The invalidity applies only to the excess; the base half is validly settled.
2
Locate the evidence for the ruling
Abu Hanifa cites that the value in manumission is stipulated by sacred law (al-qimah fi al-itq mansus 'alayha). Understand what this means: the share value is fixed by Shariah, not negotiable.
The law's estimation is considered higher than any judge's (taqdir al-shar' la yakun duna taqdir al-qadi).
3
Trace the a fortiori (qiyas awla) argument
Compare with the usurper (ghasib) case: after judgment, if the usurper settles for more than the value, it is invalid. Here, since the law's estimation is even higher than a judge's, the invalidity applies with greater reason (bil-tariq al-ula).
This is qiyas awla (a fortiori analogy); the asl is the usurper case, the far' is the manumission case.
4
Note the reasoning of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn also hold invalidity, but for them it is 'apparent' (zahir) – meaning the excess is obviously unsupported by any legal basis. No need for extension from another case.
Both agree on outcome; the khilaf is only in the method of istidlal (reasoning).
5
Extract the practical distinction
If the settlement is with a commodity of a different type (e.g., goods instead of cash), the ruling changes: it is valid however it may be, because excess does not appear across different genres.
This shows the nuance: the prohibition is tied to monetary or same genus exchanges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا : أي لا يصح الصلح في حق الزيادة من نصف قيمة العبد بالاتفاق؛ أما عندهما فظاهر، وأما عند أبي حنيفة فلأن القيمة في العتق منصوص عليها، وتقدير الشرع لا يكون دون تقدير القاضي، فلا تجوز الزيادة عليه، فإذا لم يجز صلح الغاصب عن المغصوب المتلف بما زاد على قيمته بعد القضاء فلا يجوز الصلح في هذه الصورة على أكثر من نصف قيمة العبد بالطريق الأولى؛ لأن تقدير الشرع فوق تقدير القاضي، بخلاف ما تقدم من الصورة الخلافية؛ لأنها غير منصوص عليها، وإن صالحه على عرض جاز كيفما كان؛ لما بينا أنه لا يظهر الفضل عند اختلاف الجنس. [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٤/٢] وإنما قيد بقوله: "موسراً"؛ لأنه إذا كان معسراً تجب سعاية النصف على العبد. (فتح، ملا مسكين بتوضيح من المحشي)
'No': i.e., the settlement is not valid concerning the excess over half the value of the slave by agreement; as for the two, it is apparent, and as for Abu Hanifa, because the value in manumission is stipulated by sacred law, and the estimation of the law is not lower than the estimation of the judge, so increase over it is not allowed. So if the settlement of the usurper for the destroyed usurped property for more than its value after judgment is not allowed, then settlement in this scenario for more than half the value of the slave is even more impermissible (a fortiori); because the estimation of the law is above the estimation of the judge, unlike the previous disputed scenario; because that scenario was not stipulated. But if he settles with a commodity, it is valid however it may be, because we explained that excess does not appear when the type differs. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 234/2] And he restricted it by saying 'wealthy' because if he is insolvent, the slave must work to pay half. (Fath, Mulla Miskeen with clarification from the annotator)
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a wealthy man frees a jointly owned slave and then settles with his partner for more than half the slave's value. It says there is agreement that such settlement is invalid for the excess. For Abu Hanifa, the value in manumission is fixed by sacred text (the law prescribes a value), and the law's estimation is higher than a judge's. Therefore, if settlement by a usurper for more than value after judgment is invalid, then this case is even more clearly invalid. However, if the settlement is with a commodity (different type), it is valid. The restriction to a wealthy man is because if he is insolvent, the slave must work for the half.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Manumission, freeing a slave.
موسر (musir): Wealthy, able to pay; if poor, the slave must work (s'aaya) to pay the other share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the partner did not agree.
B) Because the value is fixed by shariah and cannot be increased.
C) Because the slave is not present.
D) Because the manumission is not complete.

Key Takeaway
Any settlement for more than the fixed value in manumission is invalid because the law's estimation is final and cannot be exceeded.

Agent Settlement Liability – Guarantee and Recovery
Ruling on agent settlement liability and right of recovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when an agent (wakeel) who settles on behalf of the principal becomes personally liable and when he can recover from the principal.

Core Idea
An agent's settlement liability hinges on guarantee and the transaction type: messenger in non-compensatory, principal in compensatory.

This passage clarifies the Hanafi ruling on an agent appointed to settle disputes. The key is whether the agent guarantees payment and whether the settlement involves financial exchange or not. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion about who bears the liability and who has the right to reimbursement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Determine whether the agent was authorized (wakeel) or unauthorized (fudooli). Then identify whether the settlement is in compensatory or non-compensatory matter.
Non-compensatory: denial, silence, intentional murder, partial debt. Compensatory: property for property on confession.
2
Check for Guarantee (Daman)
Has the agent undertaken to pay (guaranteed)? If yes, liability shifts to agent; if no, principal remains liable.
The text: 'the agent is not bound... unless he guarantees it'.
3
Determine the Agent's Role
In non-compensatory matters, agent is merely a messenger (safir) – liability stays with principal. In compensatory matters, agent is considered a principal (asil) – liability attaches to agent.
Agent's role changes based on transaction nature.
4
Apply the Recovery Rule
If agent guaranteed and paid, he recovers from principal in compensatory matters. In non-compensatory, if agent guaranteed (without authorization?) he still recovers? Actually text says: if he guarantees and pays, he recovers – this applies when he settles with authorization? Need to parse carefully.
Text: 'if he guarantees and pays, he may recover from the principal' – applies whether authorized or unauthorized? The unauthorized settlement is valid only if he guarantees.
5
Distinguish from Marriage and Khul
In marriage agency, agent cannot recover if he pays. In khul (divorce by payment), same rule as settlement: if agent guarantees and pays, he recovers from the principal.
Marriage is not a financial exchange, hence different rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأدى، رجع على الموكل، أمر بالأداء عنه ليفيد الأمر فائدته ؛ إذ الصلح عنه جائز بلا أمره،
عنه رجع
بخلاف النكاح؛ لأنه لا ينفذ عليه من الأجنبي، والأمر بالخلع كالأمر بالصلح حتى يرجع على الأمر إن ضمن، وأدى . (فتح زيلعي بل يلزم الموكل إلخ كالوكيل في النكاح، غير أنه إذا ضمن ههنا وأدى على الموكل، وفي النكاح لا يرجع هذا إذا صالح عنه عن إنكار أو سكوت أو إقرار في دم عمد وفي ما لا يحمل على المعاوضة كالصلح على بعض الدين؛ لأن الوكيل في هذه الأشياء سفير ومعبر وأما إذا كان الصلح عن الموكل فيما يحمل على المعاوضة بأن كان عن مال بمال عن إقرار، فإن الوكيل يلزمه ما صالح عليه، ثم يرجع به على الموكل؛ لأن الوكيل أصيل في المعاوضة المالية، فيرجع الحقوق إليه دون الموكل فيطالب هو بالعوض دون الموكل. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٤/٢]
And whoever appoints a man as agent to settle for him, and he settles, the agent is not bound by what he settled upon unless he guarantees it, rather the principal is bound. And if he settles for him without authorization, it is valid if he guarantees the wealth. The unauthorized person: 'unless he guarantees it' i.e., unless the agent undertakes the wealth. So if he guarantees and pays, he may recover from the principal, because the order to pay for him yields its benefit; since settlement for him is permissible without his order. Unlike marriage, because it is not executed for him by a stranger. The order for khul' is like the order for settlement, so he may recover from the person who ordered if he guarantees and pays. (Fath, Zayla'i) Rather the principal is bound, like the agent in marriage, except that if he guarantees here and pays, he recovers from the principal, whereas in marriage he does not recover. This is when he settles for him regarding denial, silence, or confession in intentional murder, and in what does not admit of compensation, like settlement for part of a debt; because the agent in these matters is a messenger and interpreter. But if the settlement is for the principal in what admits of compensation, i.e., from property for property on confession, then the agent is bound by what he settled upon, then he recovers it from the principal; because the agent is a principal in financial compensation, so rights return to him rather than the principal, so he is demanded for the compensation, not the principal. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 234/2]
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability of an agent (wakeel) who conducts a settlement on behalf of the principal (muwakkil). The general rule: the agent is not personally liable for the settlement amount unless he guarantees it (i.e., undertakes to pay). If he does guarantee and pays, he can recover from the principal. If he settles without authorization (as a fudooli), the settlement is valid if he guarantees the payment, and he becomes a volunteer. The text distinguishes between settlements in matters that are not considered exchange (like denial, silence, confession in intentional murder, or partial debt settlement) where the agent is merely a messenger, and settlements in financial exchanges (property for property on confession) where the agent is treated as the principal in the transaction, so he is liable for the payment and then recovers from the principal. This is analogous to the agent in marriage, but there the agent cannot recover from the principal if he pays. For khul' (divorce by payment), the same rule as settlement applies.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakeel): An agent appointed to act on behalf of another in a specific matter.
ضمان (daman): Guaranteeing or undertaking liability for payment.
سفير (safir): Messenger or intermediary who does not assume liability.
أصيل (asil): Principal in the transaction, directly bound and entitled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a settlement for part of a debt (non-compensatory), an agent guarantees payment. After paying, can he recover from the principal?
A) Yes, because he guaranteed.
B) No, because in non-compensatory matters the agent is a messenger and the principal is liable.
C) Yes, but only if he had authorization.
D) No, because the settlement is invalid.

Key Takeaway
An agent's liability in settlement depends on whether he guarantees payment and whether the settlement is a financial exchange; in non-compensatory matters he is a mere messenger, in compensatory matters he becomes the principal.

Settlement by Agent and Unauthorized Person: Liability and Conditions
Ruling with expanded commentary on sulh by proxy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an agent or unauthorized person becomes liable for the settlement amount and apply the Hanafi distinction between messenger and principal.

Core Idea
An agent or unauthorized person is only bound by settlement if he personally guarantees the money.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq elaborates on the rules of settlement (sulh) when performed by an agent or an unauthorized person (fuduli). It clarifies when the agent is merely a messenger (safir) and when he becomes a principal (asil) liable for payment. The key turning point is the agent's guarantee (dhiman), which shifts liability from the principal to the agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is settlement (sulh) performed by an agent or fuduli. The ruling is valid (sahih) but liability for the substitute varies.
2
Locate the proof structure
The text uses analogical reasoning (qiyas) comparing settlement with marriage and khul'. It also distinguishes cases based on admission/denial and the nature of the claim.
Qiyas jaliy: sulh on property vs. sulh on homicide or debt.
3
Analyze the guarantee condition
If the agent says, "I guarantee (adman) the settlement amount," he becomes liable and must pay; then he can recover from the principal. Without guarantee, the principal is directly liable.
The fuduli can only make a binding settlement if he guarantees.
4
Note the internal hanafi ikhtilaf
There is disagreement on whether an unspecified offer 'I settle with you for one thousand' (without guarantee or delivery) is immediately effective or suspended. The preferred view is suspended pending acceptance.
This ikhtilaf is from later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirin).

Book of Settlement. Settlement in financial claims: meaning the substitute of settlement. Whoever appoints a man to settle on his behalf and he settles, the agent is not liable for what he settled upon unless he guarantees it from the principal (muwakkil). Rather, the principal is bound. If he settles without authorization, it is valid if he guarantees the money. The unauthorized person: [the agent is not liable] unless he guarantees [i.e., unless the agent undertakes the money]. So when he guarantees and pays, he recovers from the principal; the order to pay on his behalf makes the order effective, since settlement on his behalf is valid without his order. Unlike marriage, because it is not binding on him from a stranger. The order for khul' is like the order for settlement, so he can recover from the orderer if he guaranteed and paid. (Fath al-Zayli: Rather the principal is bound, etc., like the agent in marriage, except that when he guarantees here and pays, it is upon the principal, whereas in marriage he does not recover. This is if he settles on his behalf upon denial, silence, or admission in intentional homicide, and in what does not bear exchange, like settlement on part of the debt, because the agent in these matters is a messenger and conveyor. As for when the settlement on behalf of the principal is in what bears exchange, such as property for property upon admission, then the agent is bound by what he settled upon and then recovers from the principal, because the agent becomes a principal in financial exchange, so rights refer to him rather than the principal, so he is demanded for the substitute instead of the principal. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/234] And the scholar Shaykh Zayn al-Din said in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: The money is binding on the principal if it is for intentional homicide, or on part of what he claims of debt even after admission, unless the agent adds it to himself, or it is property for property upon admission. Upon denial, it does not bind him absolutely. The order for settlement is an order for guarantee, so he can recover from him if he claimed without his order, like khul', unlike the order for marriage; from a stranger without order is opposite. It has aspects: if he settles with money and guarantees, it is complete and he is a volunteer, he has nothing from the settlement for himself; rather it is for the one in whose hand it is, whether admitter or denier, except if it is for a specific thing and the defendant admits, then it is for the settlor. Similarly, if he settles with his own property, like 'this thousand of mine' or 'this slave of mine', it is valid and delivery is binding. For their validity. Likewise if he says: 'Settle with so-and-so for one thousand' and delivers it; if he does not deliver it, it is suspended: if the defendant accepts, it becomes valid and the thousand is binding; otherwise it is void. Except if he says: 'Settle with me', then there is a difference between it and 'settle with so-and-so'. The fifth: that he says: 'Settle with so-and-so for this thousand' or 'for this slave' without attribution, it is like attribution to himself. In 'I settle with you for one thousand', the scholars differed: some made it suspended, some made it effective, and the first is preferred. If the substitute is reclaimed in all the cases, or he finds it to be counterfeit or bad, he does not recover from the settlor and the claim is preferred, unless the settlor guaranteed... and Allah knows best. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/371] 'Without order' means without order of the defendant. If he guarantees the money, i.e., if the settlor guarantees the money, because what the defendant obtains is only acquittal, and regarding that, the stranger and the defendant are equal, so he becomes a principal in it if he guarantees it, like the unauthorized person in khul' if he guarantees the substitute, and he becomes a volunteer towards the defendant, as if he volunteered to pay off a debt. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/234]

[PAGE_081] Book of Settlement. Settlement in financial claims. Or he adds it to his property or says 'for one thousand' and delivers; otherwise it is suspended. If the defendant accepts it, it is valid; otherwise it is void. Meaning the thousand. Settlement. And the thousand is binding, i.e., even if he does not accept. Or he adds, etc.: i.e., the settlor adds the settlement to his own property, i.e., to his own money, by saying: 'I settle with you for this thousand of mine' or 'for this slave of mine', it is also valid, because adding to one's own property is an undertaking by him to deliver to the plaintiff, and he is able to do that, so delivery is obligatory upon him. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/234] And he said in 'Mulla Miskin': i.e., his own property, by saying: 'I settle with you for this thousand of mine' or 'this slave of mine'. If he says: 'I settle with you for this thousand' or 'for this slave' without attributing it to himself, the settlement is complete by acceptance. Or he said, etc.: i.e., the settlor says: 'I settle with you for one thousand' and does not guarantee, nor does he add it to himself, and delivers the thousand to the plaintiff, it is also valid, because delivery ensures the substitute's safety for him, so the contract is complete due to the attainment of the purpose, which is the exchanged object. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/234] Otherwise, etc.: i.e., if he does not deliver the thousand to the plaintiff but says: 'I settle with you for one thousand' without guaranteeing it or adding it to himself, the contract is suspended. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/234] Otherwise, etc.: i.e., if he does not accept it, the settlement is void, because the primary party in the contract is the defendant, since the dismissal of the lawsuit accrues to him. However, the unauthorized person becomes a principal through adding the guarantee to himself; if he does not add, he remains contracting on behalf of the demanded party, so it is suspended pending his acceptance. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/234-235]
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary explains the rules of settlement (sulh) when an agent or an unauthorized person (fuduli) acts on behalf of the principal in a financial claim. The passage type is a ruling with expanded commentary. The author states the matn: if a man appoints an agent to settle a claim and the agent settles, the agent is not liable for the settlement amount unless he personally guarantees it (dhiman). The guarantee means the agent undertakes to pay from his own pocket. If he does not guarantee, the settlement binds the principal (muwakkil), not the agent. However, if the agent settles completely without the principal's order (as a fuduli), the settlement is valid only if the agent guarantees the money. The commentary clarifies that 'unless he guarantees' refers to the agent undertaking the money. If the agent guarantees and pays, he can recover from the principal, likening it to a loan for the principal's benefit. A key comparison is made with marriage (nikah): in marriage, an agent who acts without order cannot recover from the principal because marriage is not binding on a stranger; but in settlement, since the agent's act benefits the principal by ending the dispute, it is valid and the agent can recover. Similarly, an order for khul' (divorce for compensation) is like an order for settlement. The passage then distinguishes between different types of settlement: when the settlement is upon denial, silence, or admission of intentional homicide (dim amd), or on part of a debt, the agent is merely a messenger, so the liability is on the principal. But when the settlement is an exchange of property for property upon admission, the agent becomes a principal in the exchange, thus he is liable for the substitute and then recovers from the principal. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (by Zayn al-Din ibn Nujaym) reinforces that the money is binding on the principal in cases of intentional homicide or part of a debt even after admission, unless the agent adds the property to himself (i.e., makes it his own). On denial, the settlement does not bind the principal at all. The order to settle is essentially an order to guarantee, so if the agent pays, he can recover. The passage then discusses various formulations: if the settlor says 'I settle with you for this thousand of mine' or 'my slave', it is valid and delivery is binding. If he simply says 'settle with so-and-so for one thousand' and delivers it, it is valid; if he does not deliver, it is suspended pending the defendant's acceptance. If he says 'settle with me', there is a distinction. If he says 'settle with so-and-so for this thousand' without attribution, it is like attribution to himself. There is a disagreement among scholars about saying 'I settle with you for one thousand' (without specification): some consider it suspended, others effective; and the first is preferred. If the substitute (the property given in settlement) is later reclaimed (e.g., found to be stolen), or found to be counterfeit or bad, the settlor does not recover from the defendant, and the defendant's claim is preferred unless the settlor guaranteed. Throughout, the Hanafi position is presented with internal refinements, and the relied-upon (mufta bihi) views are highlighted. The passage also explains that the unauthorized person (fuduli) becomes a principal exclusively through adding the guarantee to himself; without it, he remains a contractor on behalf of the defendant and the settlement is suspended pending the defendant's acceptance.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): An agent appointed by the principal to settle a claim; his liability depends on whether he guarantees the settlement amount.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized person who settles a claim without prior appointment; his settlement is valid only if he guarantees the money, becoming a volunteer.
الضمان (al-dhiman): The guarantee by the agent or unauthorized person to pay the substitute from his own property, making him personally liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi ruling when an unauthorized person (fuduli) settles a claim without any guarantee and without the defendant's prior acceptance?
A) The settlement is void ab initio.
B) The settlement is valid and immediately binding on the defendant.
C) The settlement is suspended pending the defendant's acceptance.
D) The settlement is valid if the fuduli delivers the substitute immediately.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sulh by proxy, the agent's guarantee (dhiman) transforms him from messenger to principal, making him liable for the substitute and giving him recourse against the principal.

Validity Conditions for Sulh: Adding Property or Delivering Amount
Explains validity conditions for sulh when settler adds property or delivers amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a sulh contract is valid, suspended, or void when the settler adds property or delivers an amount.

Core Idea
A sulh is valid if the settler adds the property to himself or delivers it; otherwise it is suspended pending defendant's acceptance.

Settlement (sulh) is a noble contract that ends disputes. However, its validity depends on how the settlement amount is handled. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies three scenarios: when the settler adds the property to himself, when he delivers it, and when he does neither. Understanding these conditions ensures that a mufti can correctly advise parties and avoid enforcing an invalid settlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a settlement contract (sulh) offered by the defendant (or a third party). The ruling depends on whether the settler adds the settlement to his own property or delivers the amount.
The text distinguishes three scenarios.
2
List the conditions for immediate validity
The settlement is immediately valid if: (a) the settler adds the property to himself (e.g., 'on my thousand'), or (b) he delivers the amount (e.g., hands over cash) even without attribution.
These conditions ensure the consideration is secured.
3
Identify the suspended scenario
If the settler says 'on a thousand' without adding to himself and without delivery, the contract is suspended (mawquf). It becomes valid only if the defendant (the principal in the contract) later approves it.
The defendant is the primary party because the dispute's removal benefits him.
4
Determine the consequence of non-approval
If the defendant does not approve the suspended settlement, it becomes void (batil). The original claim remains unaffected.
The suspended contract is like a fuduli contract until ratification.
5
Note the exception for guarantee
If the settler adds a guarantee to himself (e.g., 'I guarantee this amount'), he becomes an authorized party (asil), and the contract is not suspended even without delivery or addition.
This is an adjacent ruling mentioned at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصلح من دعوى المال أو أضاف إلى ماله أو قال على ألف وسلّم وإلا توقف فإن أجازه المدعى عليه جاز وإلا بطل. أي أضاف المصالح الصلح إلى ماله أي إلى مال نفسه بأن قال صالحتك على ألفي هذا أو على عبدي هذا جاز أيضاً؛ لأن الإضافة إلى مال نفسه التزام عنه بالتسليم إلى المدعي، وهو قادر على ذلك، فيجب عليه تسليمه. ولو قال صالحتك على هذا الألف أو على هذا العبد ولم ينسبه إلى نفسه تم الصلح بقبوله. أو قال صالحتك على ألف ولم يضمن ولم يضفه إلى نفسه وسلم الألف إلى المدعي جاز أيضاً؛ لأن التسليم يوجب سلامة العوض له، فيتم العقد لحصول المقصود. وإن لم يسلم الألف إلى المدعي بل قال صالحتك على ألف ولم يضمنه ولم يضفه إلى نفسه توقف العقد. فإن لم يجزه المدعى عليه بطل الصلح؛ لأن الأصل في العقد إنما هو المدعى عليه؛ لأن دفع الخصومة حاصل له، إلا أن الفضولي يصير أصيلاً بواسطة إضافة الضمان إلى نفسه.
Settlement from a claim of property: if he adds it to his property, or says 'on a thousand' and delivers, [it is valid]; otherwise it is suspended. If the defendant accepts it, it is valid; otherwise it is void. That is: the settler adds the settlement to his property, i.e. to his own property, by saying, 'I settle with you on this thousand of mine' or 'on this slave of mine' – it is also valid, because adding it to his property is an undertaking from him to deliver to the claimant, and he is able to do so, so delivery is obligatory upon him. If he says, 'I settle with you on this thousand' or 'on this slave' without attributing it to himself, the settlement is completed by acceptance. If he says, 'I settle with you on a thousand' without guarantee and without attributing it to himself, and delivers the thousand to the claimant, it is also valid, because delivery ensures the consideration reaches him, so the contract is completed as the objective is achieved. If he does not deliver the thousand to the claimant, but says, 'I settle with you on a thousand' without guarantee and without attributing it to himself, the contract is suspended. If the defendant does not approve it, the settlement is void, because the principal in the contract is the defendant, since the removal of litigation is for his benefit, except that a stranger becomes an authorized party by adding guarantee to himself.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the ruling on settlement (sulh) in property claims. The author states three scenarios: (1) If the settler adds the settlement to his own property (e.g., 'I settle with you on my thousand'), the settlement is valid because he undertakes delivery and is capable. (2) If he says 'on a thousand' and delivers it without attributing it to himself, it is also valid because delivery achieves the purpose. (3) If he says 'on a thousand' but neither guarantees nor delivers, the contract is suspended pending the defendant's acceptance; if rejected, it becomes void. The commentary explains that the defendant is the primary party in the contract since the benefit of ending the dispute accrues to him. However, if the settler adds a guarantee to himself, he becomes an authorized party and the contract is not suspended.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulḥ): A contract of settlement ending a dispute by mutual relinquishment of rights.
المدعى عليه (al-muddaʿā ʿalayhi): The defendant against whom the claim is made.
الفضولي (al-fuḍūlī): A person who acts without authorization on behalf of another; here, the settler when he does not add guarantee to himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a settlement (sulh) become suspended (mawquf) according to this passage?
A) When the settler adds the settlement to his own property.
B) When the settler says 'on a thousand' and delivers it.
C) When the settler says 'on a thousand' without guarantee and without delivery.
D) When the defendant accepts the settlement.

Key Takeaway
A settlement (sulh) is valid if the settler adds the property to himself or delivers it; otherwise it is suspended and requires the defendant's approval to become valid.

Defining Sulh in Debt: Wording and Critique
Defines sulh and records a critique of the wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sulh in debt and explain al-Zayla'i's objection and the commentator's defense.

Core Idea
Sulh in debt is taking part and dropping the rest – but only when the settlement is on the same type of debt.

This lesson examines the Hanafi definition of sulh (settlement) in the context of debt. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines it as 'taking part of his right and dropping the rest.' However, the Hanafi jurist al-Zayla'i objected that this wording is incomplete because it does not cover cases where the settlement involves a different genus. The commentator defends the author, arguing that the wording is elliptical and intended for brevity. Understanding this debate clarifies the precise nature of sulh in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
Extract the exact definition of sulh in debt from the text: 'taking part of his right and dropping the rest.'
This is the mufta bihi definition.
2
Understand al-Zayla'i's Critique
Al-Zayla'i argues that this definition only fits when settlement is on a portion of the same debt; if a different genus is given, it is a commutative contract, not a simple dropping of the remainder.
He proposes rewording to 'settlement on what is owed by virtue of a contract of indebtedness.'
3
Analyze the Commentator's Defense
The commentator contends the definition is correct as elliptical: the implied meaning covers all forms, and accusing the author of oversight is unwarranted.
The brevity of matn justifies the concise expression.
4
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
When settlement uses the same genus, it is ibra' (dropping part); when a different genus, it is mu'awada (exchange). Both are valid sulh.
The definition works for the first; the second is interpreted.
5
Determine the Operative Definition
The relied-upon definition is the author's, as defended by the commentator. The debate is over wording, not validity.
Mufta bihi: 'taking part and dropping the rest.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الصلح في الدين الصلح عما استحق بعقد المداينة أخذ لبعض حقه وإسقاط للباقي. قال الزيلعي: هذا سهو؛ لأنه إذا صالح عن الدين لا يكون جميع صوره استيفاء لبعض حقه وإسقاطاً للباقي، وإنما يكون كذلك أن لو وقع الصلح عن الدين على بعض الدين ألا ترى أنه لو وقع عن الدين بجنس آخر يحمل على المعاوضة، والصواب أن يقال: 'الصلح على ما استحق بعقد المداينة' إلخ. كما وقع في 'القدوري'. قلت: والذي ذكره المصنف صواب، وليس بسهو؛ لأن تقدير الكلام الصلح على شيء كائن عما استحق بعقد المداينة، ولا وجه لنسبته إلى السهو عند صحة التأويل، ولاسيما هو طالب الاختصار جداً.
Chapter of settlement in debt: Settlement regarding what is owed by virtue of a contract of indebtedness is taking part of his right and dropping the rest. Al-Zayla'i said: This is an oversight; because when one settles for a debt, not all its forms are taking part of his right and dropping the rest; rather it is like that only if the settlement for the debt is on part of the debt. Do you not see that if it is for the debt with a different genus, it is construed as a commutative contract? The correct thing is to say: 'Settlement on what is owed by virtue of a contract of indebtedness' etc., as occurs in al-Quduri. I say: What the author mentioned is correct and is not an oversight; because the implied meaning of the expression is 'settlement on something that exists by virtue of a contract of indebtedness,' and there is no ground to attribute oversight to him when interpretation is sound, especially as he is very brief.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines sulh (settlement) in the context of debt. The author states it is taking part of one's right and dropping the remainder. Al-Zayla'i objects that this definition is incomplete because it only applies when the settlement is on a portion of the same debt; if the settlement involves a different genus (e.g., cash for goods), it is actually a commutative contract (mu'awada). He suggests the wording should be 'settlement on what is owed by virtue of a contract of indebtedness' as in al-Quduri. The author (or commentator) defends the original wording, arguing that it is elliptical and meant for brevity, and that the interpretation is sound. Thus the definition is accepted as a concise expression.

Key Terms
المداينة (al-mudāyanah): A contract of indebtedness, such as a credit sale or loan.
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): Settlement: a contract ending a dispute by mutual concession.
المعاوضة (al-mu'āwaḍah): A commutative contract where each party gives something in exchange for something else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zayla'i, when does sulh in debt NOT fall under the definition of 'taking part and dropping the rest'?
A) When the settlement is on a portion of the same debt.
B) When the settlement is with a different genus.
C) When the settlement involves a third party.
D) When the debt is for a specific item.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of sulh in debt is accepted as taking part of the right and dropping the rest, with the understanding that settlement with a different genus is treated as a commutative contract.

Settlement of Debt with Conditional Release (Ibra)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional release from debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid taqyid (restriction) and invalid ta'liq (suspension) in ibra, and state the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf regarding conditional release with a future payment.

Core Idea
In ibra, taqyid is valid but ta'liq is not; thus a release conditioned on future payment is valid only if the payment occurs, according to the two.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a debtor becomes free from the remaining debt if he promises to pay half the next day on condition of release. The key distinction is between restricting the release (taqyid) and suspending it on a future event (ta'liq). Abu Yusuf holds the condition void because the debt is due, making the release absolute, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the condition valid, so release only occurs upon fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The scenario: a creditor says 'Pay half tomorrow on condition that you are free from the surplus.' What is the ruling if the debtor does not pay? The disagreement is whether the condition is valid.
Note the terminology: 'ala makes it a condition.
2
State the Hanafi position (the two) and their proof
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: The debtor does not become free unless he pays the half. Proof: Ibra can be restricted (taqyid) but not suspended (ta'liq). The word 'ala makes it a valid condition; failure to pay means the release did not occur.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his proof
Abu Yusuf: The debtor becomes free even without payment. Proof: The condition is meaningless because the debt is due and payment is obligatory at any time. Hence the suspension is void, making the release absolute.
This is a classic difference over the nature of ibra.
4
Analyze the five sub-cases
The passage lists five scenarios: (1) the original, (2) explicit restriction, (3) release on condition of future payment, (4) absolute release without time, and (5) using 'if' causing suspension. Each tests the boundary between taqyid and ta'liq.
Focus on cases 1, 2, and 5 to see the contrast.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, if the debtor pays after the specified time, according to Abu Yusuf he is already free and owes nothing; according to the two, he remains liable until the condition is met.
This highlights the importance of precise wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألف فقال : أدّ غدا نصفه على أنك بريء من الفضل، ففعل برئ، وإلا لا، أي ألف درهم، أي في الحال وهو خمس مائة الباقية، عن الفضل، وهو خمسمائة الباقية، ففعل من عليه الألف ذلك بأن أدى إليه في الغد خمسمائة برئ عن الفضل بالاتفاق؛ لأن الإبراء يحتمل التقييد بالشرط، وإن لم يحتمل التعليق به وكلمة "على" جعلت شرطاً؛ تصحيحاً لتصرفه، وإن كانت تستعمل للمعاوضة. وإلا لا: أي وإن لم يؤد غداً النصف وهو خمسمائة، لا يبرأ عندهما، وقال أبو يوسف: يبرأ وإن لم يؤد، ولا تعود إليه الخمس مائة الساقطة أبداً؛ لأن اشتراط الأداء ضائع؛ لأن النقد واجب عليه في كل زمان يطالبه هو فيه؛ إذ المال حال عليه، فبطل التعليق، فصار إبراء مطلقاً، ولهما: أنه علق الإبراء بشرط، ولم يوجد. وهذه المسألة على وجوه الأول ما ذكرنا، والثاني: أن بالتقييد بأن يقول : صالحتك عن الألف على خمس مائة تدفعها إلي غداً، وأنت بريء من الزيادة على أنك إن لم تدفعها إلي غداً فلا تبرأ من الباقي، فيكون الأمر كما قال، والثالث: إذا قال: أبراتك من خمسمائة الألف على أن تعطيني خمس مائة غداً، فحكمه أنه يبرأ من وإن لم يؤد الخمسمائة في الغد. والرابع: أن يقول: أد إلي خمسمائة على أنك بريء من باقيه، ولم يوقت للأداء وقتاً، فحكمه أنه يبرأ مطلقاً؛ لأنه إبراء مطلق، والخامس: إذا قال: إن أديت إلي خمسمائة أو إذا أديت أو متى أديت، فحكمه أنه لا تعليق بالشرط صريحاً، والبراءة لا تحتمل التعليق بالشرط؛ لما فيها من معنى التمليك. ووجه بطلان تعليق البراءة بالشرط: أن الإبراء إسقاط حتى لا يتوقف على القبول، وفيه معنى التمليك حتى يرتد بالرد، والتمليك لا يحتمل التعليق بالشرط والإسقاط يحتمل ذلك، فلمعنى التمليك قلنا: إذا صرح بالشرط لم يصح، ولمعنى الإسقاط إذا لم يصرح بالشرط يتقيد كذا في "عربي زاده".
A thousand (dirhams) so he said: Pay tomorrow half of it on condition that you are free from the surplus, so he did it, he becomes free, otherwise not. (Meaning: one thousand dirhams, meaning immediately, and it is five hundred remaining. About the surplus, and it is the remaining five hundred. So the one who owes the thousand did that by paying five hundred the next day, he becomes free from the surplus by agreement; because release (ibra) admits of restriction (taqyid) by a condition, but does not admit of suspension (ta'liq) by it. The word 'ala' makes it a condition to validate his action, although it is used for exchange. 'Otherwise not' means if he does not pay half the next day, i.e., five hundred, he does not become free according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: He becomes free even if he does not pay, and the dropped five hundred never returns to him; because stipulating performance is futile; since payment is obligatory on him at any time the creditor demands it, because the debt is due, so the suspension becomes void, and it becomes an absolute release. And according to the two: He suspended the release on a condition, and it did not occur. This issue has several aspects: The first is what we mentioned. The second: that with restriction, such as saying: I settle with you for the thousand on five hundred to be paid tomorrow, and you are free from the excess on condition that if you do not pay it tomorrow, you are not free from the remainder, then it is as he said. The third: If he says: I release you from five hundred of the thousand on condition that you give me five hundred tomorrow, then its ruling is that he is free from (the debt) even if he does not pay the five hundred tomorrow. The fourth: If he says: Pay me five hundred on condition that you are free from the rest, and he did not set a time for payment, then its ruling is that he is free absolutely, because it is an absolute release. The fifth: If he says: If you pay me five hundred, or when you pay, or whenever you pay, then its ruling is that there is no explicit suspension by a condition, and release does not admit of suspension by a condition because it contains an element of ownership. And the reason for the invalidity of suspending release on a condition is that release is a waiver such that it does not depend on acceptance, and it contains an element of ownership such that it is rejected by refusal. Ownership does not admit of suspension by a condition, while waiver does. So due to the element of ownership we say: If he explicitly states a condition, it is not valid; and due to the element of waiver, if he does not explicitly state a condition, it is restricted. Thus in 'Arabi Zada.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Ḥanafī dispute regarding the validity of conditional release in a debt compromise. The matn presents a scenario where a debtor says, 'Pay half tomorrow on condition you are free from the surplus.' If he pays, he is free; otherwise not. The commentary explains that this is agreed upon because release (ibra) can be restricted by a condition but not suspended on a future event. The word 'ala' turns it into a condition. Then it discusses the disagreement: Abu Yusuf holds that the debtor becomes free even without payment because the condition is void; the debt is already due, so the condition is meaningless, making the release absolute. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) maintain the condition is valid, so release only occurs upon fulfillment. Five sub-cases are then enumerated: (1) the original, (2) explicit restriction, (3) release on condition of future payment, (4) absolute release without time, and (5) using 'if' causing suspension which is invalid. The commentary concludes by explaining the legal theory: ibra' has aspects of both waiver (isqat) and ownership (tamlik). Waiver allows conditioning but not suspension, while ownership forbids suspension; hence explicit suspension invalidates, but implicit restriction is valid.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Release from a debt; a legal act of dropping a claim.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a legal effect on a future uncertain event.
تقييد (taqyid): Restriction of a legal effect by a condition that is part of the contract itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if a debtor says 'Pay me half tomorrow on condition you are free from the rest,' and the debtor does not pay tomorrow, what is the ruling?
A) He is free from the surplus
B) He remains liable for the surplus
C) The settlement is void
D) He is free only if he pays within a month

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a release (ibra) can be restricted by a condition (taqyid) but cannot be suspended on a future event (ta'liq); conditional release is valid only if the condition is fulfilled.
