Debtor's Conditional Acknowledgment: Ḥanafī Ruling and Analogy to Ṣulḥ
Ruling on debtor conditioning acknowledgment on deferment or reduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a debtor's conditional acknowledgment and distinguish the effect of secret vs. open declaration.

Core Idea
A debtor's conditional acknowledgment is valid and binding if made voluntarily, even under constraint.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqāʾiq discusses a scenario where a debtor conditions his acknowledgment on the creditor granting time or reducing the debt. The Ḥanafī school holds this acknowledgment valid and binding, drawing an analogy to a settlement (ṣulḥ) when the debtor denies the debt. The ruling turns on whether the debtor is coerced: if he acts voluntarily, the condition is effective. The passage also distinguishes between secret and open declarations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
The debtor says to the creditor: 'I do not acknowledge your debt upon me until you defer it or reduce some of it.' Note the debtor is not denying the debt outright but making the acknowledgment conditional.
2
Determine the Ḥanafī ruling
If the creditor agrees and defers or reduces, the acknowledgment is valid (ṣaḥḥa) and binding (lazima). The debtor benefits from the condition.
3
Analyze the analogy to ṣulḥ maʿa al-inkār
The passage explicitly draws an analogy to settlement with denial: just as a debtor can settle a disputed debt by paying something despite denying it, here the conditional acknowledgment is valid because the debtor is not coerced—he could deny or take an oath.
4
Distinguish secret vs. open declaration
If said secretly, the acknowledgment takes effect as conditioned (deferred or reduced). If said openly in front of witnesses, the acknowledgment is immediate and the debtor must pay at once, because the open declaration is seen as a full admission.
5
Note the opposing view
The three major schools (Mālikī, Shāfiʿī, Ḥanbalī) reportedly hold that such conditional acknowledgment is not valid. The Ḥanafī response rests on voluntariness and the analogy to ṣulḥ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فتح) قال لآخر إلخ: أي ومن قال لآخر : لا أقر لك بمالك علي حتى تؤخره أي مالك عني، يعني تؤجله، أو تحط عنه بعضه، ففعل الدائن ذلك، أما إن أخره أو حط عنه بعضه، صح. هذا الفعل عليه أي على الدائن، يعني إن أخره يتأخر عنه، وإن حط عنه بعضه ينحط؛ لأن المديون ليس بمكره، فصار هذا نظير الصلح مع الإنكار، وعند الثلاثة: لا يصح. ومن صح: أي لزمه، وليس للدائن أن يطالب المديون في الحال وبما حط، هذا إذا قاله سراً حتى إذا قال علانية بحضور الشهود يؤخذ المقر بالمال في الحال ملا مسكين وقال في "حاشية فتح المعين": لأنه ليس بمكره؛ لتمكنه من إقامة البيئة أو التحليف، فينكل، وهو نظير الصلح مع الإنكار؛ فكان مختاراً في تصرفه، أقصى ما في الباب أنه مضطر، لكن الاضطرار لا يمنع من نفوذ تصرفه كبيع ماله بالطعام عند المخمصة [تبيين الحقائق: ٤٩٧/٥]
(Fath) He said to another etc.: i.e., and he who says to another: I do not acknowledge your debt upon me until you defer it, i.e., your property from me, meaning you give me time, or reduce some of it, and the creditor does that, either if he gives time or reduces some of it, it is valid. This action is binding upon the creditor, meaning if he gives time, it is deferred, and if he reduces some, it is reduced; because the debtor is not coerced, so this becomes analogous to settlement with denial. According to the three, it is not valid. From 'valid' meaning it is binding, and the creditor may not demand the debtor immediately nor what was reduced. This if he said it secretly, but if he said it openly in the presence of witnesses, the person acknowledging the debt is held immediately. Mulla Miskin said in the gloss of Fath al-Mu'in: Because he is not coerced, since he can produce evidence or take an oath, causing the other to be hesitant; it is analogous to settlement with denial, so he is voluntary in his action. At most, he is constrained, but constraint does not prevent the effectiveness of his action, like selling his property for food during famine.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a scenario where a debtor says to his creditor, 'I do not acknowledge your debt upon me until you give me time (to pay) or reduce some of it.' If the creditor agrees, the action is valid and binding. The commentary explains that the debtor is not coerced (he could deny or swear), so his statement is voluntary. It is analogous to a settlement (sulh) when the debtor denies the debt, which is valid. However, the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad?) reportedly disagree, saying it is not valid. The text then clarifies that if the debtor says this secretly, it is binding; if openly in front of witnesses, the acknowledgment is immediate. The justification is that the debtor could have taken an oath or provided evidence, so he is not under compulsion. Even though he is in a difficult position, it does not invalidate his action, just as selling property during famine is valid.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or debt; a unilateral admission.
صلح مع الإنكار (sulh ma'a al-inkar): Settlement where the debtor denies the debt initially; valid in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a debtor says secretly, 'I do not acknowledge the debt unless you give me time,' and the creditor agrees, what is the ruling?
A) The acknowledgment is invalid because it is coerced.
B) The acknowledgment is valid and binding, and the creditor cannot demand immediate payment.
C) The acknowledgment is valid only if witnessed.
D) The debtor must pay immediately despite the condition.

Key Takeaway
A debtor's voluntary conditional acknowledgment is valid and binding in Hanafi law, analogous to settlement with denial, even if the debtor is in financial difficulty.

Shared Debt and Compromise by One Co-creditor
Rulings on compromise by one co-creditor in a shared debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal options of a co-creditor when the other compromises his share of a shared debt, and identify the conditions that govern the compromise.

Core Idea
Compromise by one co-creditor gives the other the choice to share the compensation or pursue the debtor.

When two people jointly own a debt against a third party, special rules govern any compromise by one of them. This lesson explains the rights of the remaining creditor and the conditions under which the compromise is effective, based on the classical Hanafi text.

Guided Path
1
Define shared debt
Identify what constitutes a shared debt (dayn mushtarak): a debt owed jointly to two or more creditors from a single cause (e.g., joint sale, inheritance).
The cause must be one – a single transaction or event.
2
State the compromise scenario
One of the two co-creditors compromises his share of the debt for a garment (or any non-currency item).
The compromise is a valid sulh contract.
3
List the partner's options
The other co-creditor has two choices: (1) pursue the debtor for his half of the debt, or (2) take half of the garment from the compromising partner.
The option exists because the garment is compensation for the debt, and the partner has a right of partnership.
4
Recognize the exception
If the compromising partner guarantees a quarter of the debt (i.e., pays the partner his share in cash), the partner loses the option and must accept the payment.
The quarter is half of the compromised share (since the debt is shared equally).
5
Note additional restrictions
Two restrictions apply: (a) the compromise must be on a debt, not on joint property; (b) the compensation must be of a different type than the debt (otherwise it's like receiving part of the debt).
If the compromise is on joint property or on the same type, the partner does not have the option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الدين المشترك دين بينهما صالح أحدهما عن نصيبه على ثوب، لشريكه أن يتبع المديون بنصفه، أو أي دين بينهما أي لشريكه الخيار أي يطالب أي بنصف الدين يأخذ نصف الثوب من شريكه إلا أن يضمن ربع الدين ولو قبض نصيبه شركه أي لأنه عوض عن دينه فيه ورجعا بالباقي على الغريم، أي الشريكان أي المديون المصالح فحينئذ لا يأخذ نصفه أحد الشريكين لشريك الآخر فصل: هذا فصل في الدين المشترك، وهو ما حصل بسبب متحد، كما لو باعا عبداً مشتركاً صفقة واحدة، أو استهلكه إنسان، أو كان الدين ميراثاً بين الورثة. (مسكين) دين بينهما إلخ [أخر الدين المشترك عن المفرد؛ لأن المثنى بعد الواحد. (فتح)] أي دين مشترك بين اثنين الشريكين، والدين المشترك أن يكون واجباً بسبب متحد كثمن المبيع، وقيمة العين المشتركة المستهلكة، وبدل القرض من المال المشترك بينهما، والموروث بين اثنين، صالح أحدهما أي أحد الشريكين المديون عن نصيبه من الدين على ثوب، فلشريكه الخيار إن شاء أن يتبع المديون بنصفه أي بنصف الدين لبقاء حصة في ذمته أو يأخذ نصف الثوب من شريكه؛ لأن له حق المشاركة؛ لأنه عوض عن دينه. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٧/٢] إلا أن يضمن إلخ: وفي بعض النسخ: يضمنه، أي يضمن الشريك المصالح ربع الدين؛ لأن حقه في الدين لا في الثوب، ولا فرق فيه بين أن يكون الصلح عن إقرار أو عن سكوت أو عن إنكار ، ثم ههنا قيدان آخران الأول: أن يكون المصالح عنه ديناً؛ لأنه لو كان الصلح عن عين مشتركة يختص المصالح ببدل الصلح، وليس لشريكه أن يشاركه فيه؛ لكونه معاوضة من كل وجه؛ لأن المصالح. عنه مال حقيقة بخلاف الدين والثاني: أن يكون المصالح عليه ثوباً، والمراد خلاف جنس الدين؛ لأنه لو صالحه على جنسه يشاركه فيه، ويرجعان على المديون، وليس للقابض فيه خيار ؛ لأنه بمنزلة قبض بعض الدين رمز الحقائق: ۲۳۷/۲] ولو قبض نصيبه إلخ: أي ولو قبض أحد الشريكين نصيبه شركه الشريك الآخر. (مسكين) وقال في "الفتح": لأن قسمة الدين لا تتصور والمقبوض بدل عنه فله أن يشاركه فيه؛ لكونه عين حقه من وجه، بخلاف ما إذا اشترى به ثوباً حيث لا يشاركه فيه؛ لأنه مبادلة من كل وجه، وإنما كان له أن يشاركه في الثوب في فصل الصلح كي لا يلزم المصالح الضرر ولو سلم المقبوض واختار متابعة الغريم ثم مات الغريم، رجع على القابض بنصف ما قبض؛ لأن التسليم مقيد بشرط سلامة الباقي، لكن ليس له أن يرجع في عين تلك الدراهم المقبوضة؛ لأن حقه فيها قد سقط بالتسليم فلا يعود حقه فيها بالتوى، ويعود إلى ذمة في مثلها كذا في "الزيلعي"، ومنه يعلم ما في "العيني" من الخلل. فتح المعين ورجعا إلخ : أي الشريكان بالباقي من الدين على الغريم، وهو المديون؛ لأن قسمة الدين لا تتصوّر في الذمة قبل القبض والقبض بدل عنه، فله أن يشاركه فيه إن شاء؛ لأنه . حقه عين
Chapter: Shared debt. A debt between them, one of them compromised from his share for a garment, his partner has the option to follow the debtor for half of it, or to take half of the garment from his partner unless he guarantees a quarter of the debt. And even if he received his share, his partner shares it. Because it is compensation for his debt. And both of them return with the remainder upon the debtor, the two partners, the debtor, the compromising partner. So then one of the partners does not take half of it for the other partner. Chapter: This is a chapter on shared debt, which is what arises from a single cause, like if they both sold a joint slave in one transaction, or a person destroyed it, or the debt is inheritance among heirs. (Miskin) A debt between them etc. [He deferred the shared debt after the singular because dual comes after singular. (Fath)] i.e., a shared debt between two partners, and shared debt is what is obligatory due to a single cause, like the price of sold goods, the value of a joint property that was destroyed, the substitute of a loan from joint property between them, and inherited between two. One of them compromised, i.e., one of the two partners, the debtor, from his share of the debt for a garment. His partner has the option: if he wishes to follow the debtor for half of it, i.e., half of the debt because his share remains in the debtor's liability, or to take half of the garment from his partner because he has the right of partnership, since it is compensation for his debt. [Rasm al-Haqa'iq: 2/237] Except that he guarantees, etc.: In some copies: he guarantees it, i.e., the compromising partner guarantees a quarter of the debt because his right is in the debt not in the garment. And there is no difference here whether the compromise is on acknowledgment, silence, or denial. Then there are two other restrictions: First, what is compromised must be a debt; because if it were a compromise on joint property, the compromiser would be entitled to the compensation exclusively, and his partner cannot share it because it is an exchange from every aspect, since what is compromised is actual property unlike a debt. Second, what is compromised upon must be a garment, meaning a different type from the debt; because if he compromises on the same type, the partner shares it, and both return upon the debtor, and the recipient has no option because it is like receiving part of the debt. [Rasm al-Haqa'iq: 2/237] And even if he received his share, etc.: i.e., even if one of the partners received his share, his other partner shares it. (Miskin) And it is said in al-Fath: Because division of a debt is not conceivable, and what is received is in lieu of it, so he may share it, since it is a representation of his right from one aspect, unlike if he purchased a garment with it, where he does not share it because it is an exchange from every aspect. He only has the right to share the garment in the chapter of compromise so that the compromising partner is not harmed. And if he delivered what was received and chose to follow the debtor, then the debtor died, he returns upon the recipient for half of what he received; because the delivery is restricted by the condition of the safety of the remainder, but he may not return to the specific coins received because his right in them has fallen by delivery; it does not return with the loss, and it returns to a liability of the like. Thus in al-Zayla'i, and from it one knows the flaw in al-Ayni. Fath al-Mu'in: And both return, etc.: i.e., the two partners (return) upon the debtor for the remainder of the debt; because division of a debt is not conceivable in the liability before receipt, and the receipt is in lieu of it, so he may share it if he wishes, because it is his right in a specific form.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This chapter deals with the rules of shared debt (dayn mushtarak) where two creditors have a joint claim on a single debtor due to a common cause (e.g., joint sale, inheritance). The matn states: if one of two co-creditors compromises his share of the debt for a garment, the other co-creditor has two options: (1) pursue the debtor for his half of the debt, or (2) take half of the garment from his compromising partner. However, the compromising partner can avoid this by guaranteeing a quarter of the debt (i.e., paying the partner his share in cash). The commentary explains that this is because the garment is compensation for the debt, and the partner has a right of participation. It then discusses two restrictions: the compromise must be on a debt (not joint property), and the compensation must be of a different type than the debt (otherwise it would be like receiving part of the debt, eliminating the partner's option). Also, if one co-creditor receives his share directly from the debtor, the other partner shares it because division of a debt is not possible before receipt. If the partner chooses to follow the debtor and the debtor dies, the partner can recover half from the other partner who received payment, but not the specific coins. Both partners then return upon the debtor for the remainder.

Key Terms
دين مشترك (dayn mushtarak): A debt owed jointly to two or more creditors arising from a single cause.
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement to resolve a dispute, often by exchanging or reducing claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two people have a shared debt of 1000 dirhams on someone, and one creditor compromises his share for a garment worth 500, what are the other creditor's options?
A) He must accept half of the garment.
B) He may either pursue the debtor for his 500 share or take half of the garment from his partner.
C) He can only pursue the debtor for 500.
D) He can demand the entire garment from his partner.

Key Takeaway
In a shared debt, compromise by one creditor gives the other the choice to either pursue the debtor for his share or share the compensation, unless the compromising partner guarantees a portion of the debt.

Definition and Ruling of Wadi'ah (Deposit)
Defines wadi'ah linguistically and legally along with its ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of wadi'ah, identify its pillars and conditions, and apply the ruling that the depositary is only liable for breach of trust (mighl).

Core Idea
The depositary is not liable for accidental loss—only for breach of trust.

Wadi'ah (deposit) is a ubiquitous contract in daily life—lending a book, parking a car with a valet, or leaving luggage with a friend. Yet many misunderstand the trustee's liability. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq defines wadi'ah linguistically and legally, outlines its essential pillar and conditions, and establishes the core Hanafi ruling: the depositary holds the property as a trust (amanah) and is only accountable for deliberate breach or negligence, not accidental loss.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic and legal definitions
In language, al-ida' is simply entrusting another with preservation. Legally, wadi'ah is a specific type of trust (amanah) created by contract, where the depositee is not liable for loss unless he breaches the trust.
The term wadi'ah (with ta') is a noun denoting the deposited property itself.
2
Identify the pillar (rukn)
The contract's pillar is offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul), which may be explicit (words) or implicit (actions indicating acceptance of preservation).
For example, picking up a lost waterskin implies acceptance only if the owner is absent.
3
List the conditions (shurut)
Two conditions are highlighted: (1) the property must be physically possessable (e.g., not a runaway slave or submerged item); (2) the depositor must be mukallaf (legally responsible) for the obligation of preservation to arise.
If a minor deposits, the depositee has no duty to preserve and is not liable for destruction.
4
State the ruling (hukm) and obligations
The ruling is wajib (obligatory) preservation of the deposit, and the property becomes a trust (amanah). Additionally, the depositee must return the deposit upon demand. Accepting a deposit is sunnah (recommended).
The obligation arises only after valid acceptance.
5
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The hadith of 'Ali ("The depositary is not liable except for breach of trust") proves that liability is limited to cases of treachery (mighl) or negligence, not accidental loss. This is a zanni al-thubut dalil with qati dalalah.
The Hanafi school applies this principle strictly, distinguishing wadi'ah from general amanah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوديعة
تعريف الوديعة وحكمه
كتاب الوديعة
الإيداع تسليط الغير على حفظ ماله والوديعة ما يترك عند الأمين، في الشرع أمانة،
وهي الوديعة في يد المودع أمانة يصح،
أمانة كتاب الوديعة إلخ: هي تركت للحفظ، والمضاربة للاسترباح، فكانت أكثر وجوداً من الوديعة، فلهذا أخرت عنها، ثم الوديعة والإيداع في اللغة: تسليط الغير على الحفظ أيّ شيء كان مالاً أو غيره يقال: أودعت زيداً مالاً واستودعته إياه إذا دفعته إليه للحفظ، فأنا مودع ومستودع بالكسر فيهما، وزيد مودع ومستودع بالفتح فيهما، والمال مودع ووديعة، قال في "الفتح": وفيه أي في كون الوديعة في اللغة تسليط الغير إلخ تأمل؛ لأن الوديعة كما في "الطلبة": المال المتروك عند إنسان لحفظه، فعيلة من الودع وهو الترك والإيداع والاستيداع بمعنى وركنها: الإيجاب قولاً أو فعلاً صريحاً أو كناية، والقبول صريحاً أو دلالة في حق الحفظ. وشرطها كون المال قابلاً لإثبات اليد ليتمكن من حفظه حتى لو أودعه الآبق أو المال الساقط في البحر لا وكون المودع مكلفاً شرط لوجوب الحفظ عليه حتى لو أودع صبياً، فاستهلكها لم يضمن، ولو كان عبداً محجوراً ضمن بعد العتق، وحكمها: وجوب الحفظ وصيرورة المال أمانة عنده، قال في "الفتح": بقي من حكمها وجوب الأداء عند الطلب واستحباب قبولها. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٥/٧] وسببها تعلق البقاء المقدر بتعاطيها من حيث محاسنها: بذل منافع بدنه وماله في إعانة عباد الله واستيجابه الأجر والثناء. (ملا مسكين، فتح ملتقطاً)
ومن الوديعة إلخ كان القياس أن يقول: كتاب الوديع بدون التاء؛ لأنه فعيل بمعنى مفعول وفيه يستوي المذكر والمؤنث، تقول: رجل جريح وامرأة جريح وإنما عدل عن القياس؛ لأنه جعل من عداد الأسماء تدخل عليه التاء كالذبيحة والنطيحة فتكون للنقل لا للتأنيث "نوح آفندي". (فتح)
التعاضد، الإيداع إلخ: أي الإيداع في الشرع تسليط الغير على حفظ ماله والوديعة اسم لما يترك مطلقاً في اللغة، وفي الشرع: هي ما يترك عند الأمين حتى إذا هلكت في يده لا يضمن؛ لقوله علي: "ليس على المودع غير المغل ضمان"، رواه الدار قطني، والمغل الخائن. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٥١/٢]
تسليط الغير إلخ: أي صريحاً أو دلالة، بأن انفتق زق رجل، فأخذه رجل ثم تركه، ولم يكن المالك حاضراً، يضمن؛ لأنه لما أخذه، فقد التزم حفظه دلالة، ولو لم يأخذه لا يضمن، وإن كان المالك حاضراً، لا يضمن في الوجهين. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٤/٧] (فتح)
أمانة إلخ من حمل العام على الخاص وهو جائز ك إنسان حيوان" بخلاف عكسه؛ لأن الوديعة عبارة كون الشيء أمانة باستحقاق صاحبه عند غيره قصداً، والأمانة قد تكون من غير قصد. (فتح)
أمانة وهي إلخ أي الوديعة أمانة في يد المودع، فلا يضمن بالهلاك، قال في "البحر": والفرق بين الوديعة والأمانة من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الوديعة خاص بما ذكرناه، والأمانة خاصة بما لو وقع في يده شيء من غير قصده بأن هبت الريح بثوب إنسان، وألقته في حجر غيره، وحكمهما مختلف في بعض الصور؛ لأن في الوديعة يبرأ عن الضمان
Chapter of Deposit. Definition of Deposit and its Ruling. Chapter of Deposit. Al-Ida' (depositing) is entrusting another with the preservation of one's property. And al-Wadi'ah (the deposit) is what is left with a trustee; in Sacred Law, it is a trust. And the deposit in the hand of the depositee is a trust, and it is valid. (Commentary) The chapter of deposit: it is left for preservation, while mudarabah is for profit-making. So deposit is more prevalent than mudarabah, therefore it was placed after it. Then al-wadi'ah and al-ida' linguistically: entrusting another with preservation of anything, whether property or otherwise. One says: 'I deposited property with Zayd' and 'I entrusted him with it' when one hands it over to him for preservation. So I am a depositor (with kasra), and Zayd is a depositee (with fatha). The property is called mawda' and wadi'ah. He said in al-Fath: 'In it (i.e., the idea that wadi'ah linguistically is entrusting another etc.) there is consideration, because wadi'ah, as in al-Talibah, is property left with a person for its preservation; it is fa'ilah from wada'a which means leaving. And al-ida' and al-istida' have the same meaning. Its pillar: offer (ijab) verbally or by action, explicit or implicit, and acceptance (qabul) explicit or by indication in the context of preservation. Its condition: the property must be capable of being possessed so that he can preserve it, so if he deposits a runaway slave or property fallen into the sea, it is not (valid). And the depositor must be legally responsible (mukallaf); this is a condition for the obligation of preservation, so if a minor makes a deposit and the depositee destroys it, he is not liable. And if a restricted slave (makes a deposit) he becomes liable after emancipation. Its ruling: the obligation of preservation and the property becoming a trust in his hand. He said in al-Fath: 'What remains of its ruling is the obligation of return upon demand and the recommendation of acceptance.' (al-Bahr al-Raiq 7:395). Its cause (sabab) is the connection of the intended continuation with the transaction in terms of its merits: spending one's physical and financial benefits in helping Allah's servants and deserving reward and praise. (Mulla Miskin, Fath Multipurpose). 'Of the deposit' etc.: the analogical rule would be to say 'Kitab al-Wadi' (without the ta') because it is fa'il in the sense of maf'ul and in it masculine and feminine are equal. One says 'rajul jarih' and 'imra'ah jarih'. It departed from analogy because it was made in the category of nouns that take the ta' like al-dhabiha and al-natiha, so it is for transference (naql) not for feminization. (Nuh Afandi, Fath). al-Ta'addud? Al-ida' etc.: i.e., al-ida' in Sacred Law is entrusting another with the preservation of one's property, and al-wadi'ah is the name for what is left absolutely linguistically. In Sacred Law: it is what is left with a trustee, so if it perishes in his hand, he is not liable, because of the saying of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The depositary is not liable except for breach of trust', narrated by al-Daraqutni. And al-mighl is the treacherous one. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 2:251). 'Entrusting another' etc.: i.e., explicitly or by indication. If a man's waterskin split, and someone took it then left it, and the owner was not present, he is liable because when he took it, he implicitly committed to its preservation. If he had not taken it, he is not liable. And if the owner was present, he is not liable in both cases. (al-Bahr al-Raiq 7:394) (Fath). 'A trust...' etc.: this is applying the general to the specific, which is permissible like 'man is an animal', unlike its opposite. Because al-wadi'ah is the state of a thing being a trust by its owner's entitlement with someone intentionally, while trust (amanah) may be unintentional. (Fath). 'A trust... i.e., the deposit is a trust in the hand of the depositee, so he is not liable for destruction. He said in al-Bahr: 'The difference between wadi'ah and amanah is from two aspects: one is that wadi'ah is specific to what we mentioned, and amanah is specific to when something comes into one's hand unintentionally, like the wind blowing someone's garment and dropping it into another's lap. Their rulings differ in some cases because in wadi'ah he is free from liability.'
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq with its commentary defines the chapter of Wadi'ah (Deposit). The base matn states: 'Al-Ida' is entrusting another with the preservation of one's property. Al-Wadi'ah is what is left with a trustee; in Sacred Law it is a trust. The deposit in the hand of the depositee is a trust, and it is valid.' The commentary expands: The chapter on deposit is placed after mudarabah because deposit is for preservation while mudarabah is for profit, and deposit is more prevalent. Linguistically, wadi'ah is derived from wada'a (to leave). The term 'wadi'ah' is used for the property itself. The pillar (rukn) of the contract is offer and acceptance, which can be explicit or implicit by action. The condition is that the property must be tangible so the depositee can physically possess it; thus, depositing a runaway slave or property in the sea is invalid because possession is impossible. Another condition is that the depositor must be mukallaf (legally responsible) for the obligation of preservation to arise; if a minor deposits something and the depositee destroys it, there is no liability because the minor is not obligated to preserve. However, if a restricted slave deposits, he becomes liable after emancipation (because then obligation arises). The ruling (hukm) is: the depositee must preserve the property, and it becomes a trust (amanah) in his hand. He is also obligated to return it when demanded, and accepting a deposit is recommended (mustahabb) but not obligatory. The sabab (cause) of the contract is the social benefit of helping others. The commentary also explains a grammatical point: the word 'wadi'ah' has a ta' marbutah, though the analogy would be 'wadi' without it; it is treated as a name like 'dhabihah' (slaughtered animal). The proof that the depositary is not liable for accidental loss is the hadith of 'Ali: 'The depositary is not liable except for breach of trust.' The commentary then distinguishes between explicit acceptance and implicit (dalalah): if someone picks up a lost waterskin and leaves it without the owner present, he becomes liable because he implicitly accepted the trust; if he didn't pick it up, he is not liable. If the owner is present, he is not liable in either case. Finally, it distinguishes wadi'ah from general amanah: wadi'ah is an intentional trust created by contract, while amanah can arise unintentionally (like wind blowing a garment into one's lap); their rulings differ in some cases (e.g., liability for negligence).

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadīʿah): Property left with a trustee for safekeeping; it becomes a trust (amanah) in his hand, and he is not liable for accidental loss.
الإيداع (al-īdāʿ): The act of depositing: entrusting another with the preservation of one's property.
أمانة (amānah): A thing held in trust; the legal characterization of property in the depositee's hand, meaning he is not liable for loss unless negligent or in breach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following would be a valid deposit (wadi'ah)?
A) A fugitive slave
B) A car
C) Property at the bottom of the sea
D) A bird in flight

Key Takeaway
The depositary is only liable for breach of trust (mighl) or negligence, not for accidental loss, because the deposit is a trust (amanah).

Definition and Ruling of Wadi'a (Deposit)
Defines wadi'a linguistically and legally, states its ruling as amana, and provides the hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of wadi'a, identify its pillars and conditions, and explain why the depositary is not liable for loss unless treacherous or negligent.

Core Idea
A deposit is a trust: the depositary is not liable for loss except in cases of treachery or negligence.

Depositing property for safekeeping is a widespread social practice. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the wadi'a contract, outlines its essential elements, and establishes the fundamental ruling that it is an amana (trust). Understanding this contract clarifies the rights and duties of both depositor and depositary, and prevents legal disputes over liability for loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the contract of wadi'a? What is its ruling? From the passage, wadi'a is a deposit placed with a trustee, and its ruling is that it becomes an amana (trust) in the depositary's hands. Preservation is wajib upon acceptance.
The passage uses the term 'أمانة' to describe the deposit's legal nature.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? The hadith of 'Ali (ra): 'There is no liability on the depositary except the treacherous' (narrated by al-Daraqutni). This is the primary textual evidence.
The hadith is mursal (from a Tabi'i to the Prophet) but accepted by Hanafis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The phrase 'ليس عليه ضمان' negates liability, and 'غير المغل' excepts the treacherous, implying that the default is no liability.
The word 'المغل' means the treacherous one who misappropriates.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Is there disagreement? Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold the depositary liable if he does not keep the deposit in a proper place. The Hanafi school limits liability to treachery or negligence.
The passage implies this ikhtilaf through its emphasis on the hadith.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Which view is mufta bihi? The Hanafi position (no liability without trespass or negligence) is the relied-upon view in zahir al-riwayah.
The passage does not explicitly state tarjih, but the hadith is taken as decisive.

كتاب الوديعة. تعريف الوديعة وحكمه. الإيداع تسليط الغير على حفظ ماله. والوديعة ما يترك عند الأمين. في الشرع أمانة. وهي الوديعة في يد المودع. يصح. أمانة کتاب الوديعة إلخ: هي تركت للحفظ، والمضاربة للاسترباح، فكانت أكثر وجوداً من الوديعة، فلهذا أخرت عنها، ثم الوديعة والإيداع في اللغة: تسليط الغير على الحفظ أي شيء كان مالاً أو غيره يقال: أودعت زيداً مالاً واستودعته إياه إذا دفعته إليه للحفظ، فأنا مودع ومستودع بالكسر فيهما، وزيد مودع ومستودع بالفتح فيهما، والمال مودع ووديعة، قال في "الفتح": وفيه أي في كون الوديعة في اللغة تسليط الغير إلخ تأمل؛ لأن الوديعة كما في "الطلبة": المال المتروك عند إنسان لحفظه، فعيلة من الودع وهو الترك والإيداع والاستيداع بمعنى وركنها: الإيجاب قولاً أو فعلاً صريحاً أو كناية، والقبول صريحاً أو دلالة في حق الحفظ. وشرطها كون المال قابلاً لإثبات اليد ليتمكن من حفظه حتى لو أودعه الآبق أو المال الساقط في البحر لا وكون المودع مكلفاً شرط لوجوب الحفظ عليه حتى لو أودع صبياً، فاستهلكها لم يضمن، ولو كان عبداً محجوراً ضمن بعد العتق، وحكمها: وجوب الحفظ وصيرورة المال أمانة عنده، قال في "الفتح": بقي من حكمها وجوب الأداء عند الطلب واستحباب قبولها. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٥/٧] وسببها تعلق البقاء المقدر بتعاطيها من حيث محاسنها: بذل منافع بدنه وماله في إعانة عباد الله واستيجابه الأجر والثناء. (ملا مسكين، فتح ملتقطاً) ومن الوديعة إلخ كان القياس أن يقول: كتاب الوديع بدون التاء؛ لأنه فعيل بمعنى مفعول وفيه يستوي المذكر والمؤنث، تقول: رجل جريح وامرأة جريح وإنما عدل عن القياس؛ لأنه جعل من عداد الأسماء تدخل عليه التاء كالذبيحة والنطيحة فتكون للنقل لا للتأنيث "نوح آفندي". [رمز الحقائق: ٢٥١/٢] تسليط الغير إلخ: أي صريحاً أو دلالة، بأن انفتق زق رجل، فأخذه رجل ثم تركه، ولم يكن المالك حاضراً، يضمن؛ لأنه لما أخذه، فقد التزم حفظه دلالة، وهي أمانة إلخ من حمل العام على الخاص وهو جائز ك إنسان حيوان" بخلاف عكسه؛ لأن الوديعة عبارة كون الشيء أمانة باستحقاق صاحبه عند غيره قصداً، والأمانة قد تكون من غير قصد. Book of Deposit. Definition of deposit and its ruling. Iida' (depositing) is enabling another to preserve one's property. And al-wadi'a (the deposit) is what is left with the trustee. In the Shari'a it is a trust. And it is the deposit in the hand of the depositary. It is valid. The book of deposit etc.: It was left for preservation, and mudaraba for profit, so it is more existent than deposit, therefore it was delayed after it. Then deposit and iida' linguistically: enabling another to preserve whatever it is, property or otherwise. It is said: I deposited property with Zayd and I entrusted him with it when I gave it to him for preservation. So I am a depositor (mudi' and mustawdi' with kasra in both), and Zayd is a depositary (muda' and mustawda' with fatha in both), and the property is muda' and wadi'a. He said in al-Fath: In it, i.e., in the linguistic definition of wadi'a as enabling another etc., there is consideration; because wadi'a, as in al-Tulba, is property left with a person for its preservation, a fa'ila pattern from al-wad' meaning leaving, and iida' and istida' have the same meaning. Its pillars: offer verbally or by action, explicitly or implicitly, and acceptance explicitly or by indication regarding preservation. Its condition: the property must be capable of being possessed so that he can preserve it; so if he deposits a runaway slave or property fallen into the sea, it is not valid. And the depositor must be legally responsible (mukallaf) as a condition for the obligation of preservation upon him; so if a child deposits and the deposit is destroyed, he is not liable; if a restricted slave deposits, he is liable after manumission. Its ruling: obligation of preservation and that the property becomes a trust with him. He said in al-Fath: There remains from its ruling the obligation of returning upon demand and the recommendation of accepting the deposit. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:395] Its cause relates to the estimated permanence of engaging in it from the perspective of its virtues: spending the benefits of his body and property in aiding the servants of Allah and earning reward and praise. (Mulla Miskin, Fath mulattaqatan) And from al-wadi'a etc., the analogy would be to say 'kitab al-wadi' without the ta', because it is fa'il in the sense of maf'ul, and in it masculine and feminine are equal; you say: rajul jarih and imra'a jarih. But they deviated from analogy because it was made as one of the nouns that take the ta' like al-dhabiha and al-natihat, so it is for transference not for femininity. (Nuh Afandi) (Fath) al-ta'adul, al-iida' etc.: i.e., al-iida' in the Shari'a is enabling another to preserve his property, and al-wadi'a is a name for what is left absolutely in language, and in the Shari'a: it is what is left with the trustee so that if it perishes in his hand, he is not liable; because of his (Ali's) statement: 'There is no liability on the depositary except the treacherous', narrated by al-Daraqutni, and 'al-mughall' is the treacherous. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:251] Enabling another etc.: i.e., explicitly or by indication, such as a man's leather bag burst, and another man took it then left it, and the owner was not present: he is liable; because when he took it, he implicitly undertook its preservation. And it is a trust etc., from carrying the general to the specific, which is permissible, like 'insan hayawan', unlike its opposite; because wadi'a is an expression of a thing being a trust by deserving of its owner with another intentionally, while trust may be unintentional. (Fath) Trust indeed etc.: i.e., the wadi'a is a trust in the hand of the depositary, so he is not liable for destruction. He said in al-Bahr: The difference between wadi'a and amana is in two respects: one: that wadi'a is specific to what we mentioned, and amana is specific to what falls into his hand unintentionally, like when the wind blows a person's garment and throws it into another's lap, and their rulings differ in some cases; because in wadi'a he is absolved of liability, so he is not liable for destruction, and the depositary may keep it himself or with his dependents, but if he keeps it with someone else...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof defining the wadi'a (deposit) contract and its basic legal ruling. The author begins by giving the linguistic and legal definitions of wadi'a and iida'. Linguistically, it means entrusting another with preservation; legally, it is a trust (amana) left with a reliable person, so that if it perishes in his hands without negligence, he is not liable. The proof is the hadith 'There is no liability on the depositary except the treacherous' (narrated by al-Daraqutni). The pillar (rukn) of the contract is offer and acceptance, which can be explicit or implicit. Conditions include that the property must be capable of being physically possessed and that the depositor must be legally responsible (mukallaf). The ruling includes the obligation to preserve the deposit and to return it upon demand; accepting a deposit is recommended (mustahabb). The passage then distinguishes between wadi'a (specific to a contract of deposit) and amana (general trust that may arise without a contract). The Hanafi position is that the depositary is not liable for destruction unless he is treacherous (mughall) or negligent. The depositary may keep the deposit himself or with his household (iyal), but if he gives it to a stranger, he becomes liable if it perishes.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'a): Property left with a trustee for preservation; a specific type of trust (amana) created by contract.
أمانة (amana): A trust; property held without liability for loss unless caused by negligence or treachery.
المودع (al-mudi'): The depositor; the person who gives the property for safekeeping.
المودع بالفتح (al-muda' bi'l-fath): The depositary; the person who receives the property for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The depositary is not liable for loss of a deposit unless he is treacherous or negligent; the deposit is a trust, not a guarantee.

Preservation of Deposit and Liability Rules
Expands on preservation rules and liability conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on preservation of a deposit, define 'iyal (household), and determine when a depositary becomes liable for loss.

Core Idea
The depositary may keep the deposit with his household; giving it to a stranger without necessity incurs liability.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the rules governing the preservation of a deposit (wadi'a). It clarifies the key distinction between wadi'a and amana, establishes the general principle of non-liability for the depositary, and details the conditions under which depositing with another person transfers liability. Understanding these rulings is essential for anyone entrusted with someone else's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of non-liability
The depositary is not liable for loss of the deposit, whether preventable or not, based on the hadith 'There is no liability on the depositary except the treacherous.'
2
Define 'iyal (household)
'Iyal refers to those who reside with the depositary actually or legally, not those he supports financially. Include wife and minor children capable of preservation; exclude hired workers not residing with him.
Legal residence: if he gives to his wife or child in another locality, they are still considered 'iyal if they are his dependents legally.
3
Apply the condition of trustworthiness
If the depositary gives the deposit to a household member who is untrustworthy and he knows this, he becomes liable for loss. Otherwise, he is not liable.
This condition is mentioned in al-Khulasa and al-Nihaya.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on redeposit
Abu Hanifa holds that if the depositary redeposits with another and separates from the deposit, he is liable. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold he is not liable if he had a valid reason. The fatwa follows Abu Hanifa's view.
If the depositary does not separate (e.g., remains in possession jointly), no liability.
5
Apply rulings to scenarios
Consider cases like giving to an agent, changing storage location, or returning to owner's dependents. The depositary may give to an agent or trusted person even if not in his household according to the fatwa.
If owner forbids giving to certain household members, the depositary must comply or be liable.

حفظ الوديعة وأحكامه. أي المودع بالفتح أي بهلاك الوديعة. الوديعة الأمناء أي بغير من عياله إذا عاد إلى الوفاق، وفي الأمانة لا يبرأ عن الضمان بعد الخلاف الثاني: أن الأمانة عام لما هو غير مضمون، فيشمل جميع الصور التي لا ضمان فيها كالعارية والمستأجر والموصى بخدمته في يد الموصى له، والوديعة ما وضع للأمانة بالإيجاب والقبول فكانا متغايرين، واختاره صاحب "النهاية" ونقل الأول عن الإمام بدر الدين الكردري. البحر الرائق: ٣٩٥/٧] وقال في ملا" مسكين: هي أمانة إلا أن الفرق بينهما أن الوديعة خاصة والأمانة عامة انتهى. قال في "الفتح" على هذا القول: فإنها أي الأمانة قد تكون بغير عقد، فعلى هذا يكون بينهما العموم والخصوص، وهذا الفرق الذي ذكره الشارح موافق للفرق الثاني الذي عزاه في "البحر" إلى "النهاية"، وأما على ما ذكره في "البحر" أولا" وعزاه إلى الكردي فيكون بينهما التباين. فلا يضمن إلخ: أي لما كانت الوديعة أمانة لا يضمن المودع بالفتح بهلاك الوديعة عنده مطلقاً، سواء هلكت بما يمكن التحرز عنه أم لا، وسواء هلكت وحدها بدون هلاك مال المودع أو لا، وكذا لا يضمن إذا سرقت؛ لقوله عليه: "ليس على المودع غير المغل "ضمان" والمغل الخائن والإغلال الخيانة. (فتح) وللمودع إلخ لأنه يحفظها بما يحفظ به ماله والمراد بالعيال من يسكن معه حقيقة أو حكماً لا من يمونه، فدخل فيهم الزوجة؛ فإن له أن يدفعها إلى زوجه، وخرج الأجير الذي لا يسكن معه، وإنما قلنا: "أو حكما"؛ لأنه لو دفعها إلى الولد الصغير وزوجته، وهما في محلة أخرى لا يضمن ولو كان لا يجيء إليهما ولا ينفق عليهما، لكن يشترط في الصغير أن يكون قادراً على الحفظ كذا في "الخلاصة"، ويشترط أن يكون من في عياله أميناً؛ لأنه لو دفع إلى زوجته وهي غير أمينة، وهو عالم بذلك، أو تركها في بيته الذي فيه ودائع الناس وذهب، فضاعت ضمن، في "الخلاصة" و"النهاية"، وظاهر المتون أن كون الغير في عياله شرط، واختاره في "الخلاصة"، وقال: والأبوان كالأجنبي حتى يشترط كونهما في عياله. كذا واختار صاحب "النهاية" تبعاً لغيره عدم الاشتراط، وقال: وعليه الفتوى حتى جوز الدفع إلى وكيله، أو أمين من أمنائه وليس في عياله، أو شريكه مفاوضة أو عناناً، وفي "الخلاصة": لمن في عياله أن يدفع إلى من في عياله، ولو نهاه عن الدفع إلى بعض من في عياله فدفع، إن لم يجد بدأ من الدفع لا يضمن، وإلا ضمن، ولو قال له: احفظها في هذا البيت فحفظها في بيت آخر من تلك الدار لا يضمن إلا إذا كان ظهر البيت المنهي عنه إلى السكة، فحينئذ يضمن كما لو قال له احفظها في هذه الدار ، فحفظها في دار أخرى، فإنه يضمن إلا إذا كانت الدار الأخرى مثل الدار الأولى، أو أحرز منها فإنه لا يضمن وسيأتي بتمامه. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٥/٧] فإن حفظها إلخ: أي إن حفظها بغير من في عياله ضمن فأفاد أن المودع لا يودع، فإن أودع، فهلكت عند الثاني، إن لم يفارق الأول لا ضمان على واحد منهما، وإن فارقه ضمن الأول عند أبي حنيفة محله، ولا يضمن الثاني، وإن أودع بلا إذن، ثم أجاز المالك خرج الأول من البين، كذا "الخلاصة"، والرد إلى عيال المالك =
Preservation of the deposit and its rulings. That is, the depositary (with fatha), i.e., upon destruction of the deposit. The deposit of trustees, i.e., with other than his dependents when he returns to agreement, and in the case of trust (amana) he is not absolved from liability after the second difference: that amana is general for what is not guaranteed, so it includes all cases where there is no liability like 'ariya (loan for use), and the hired item, and the one bequeathed in service in the hand of the legatee, and wadi'a is what is placed as a trust by offer and acceptance, so they are different. And the author of al-Nihaya chose this, and he transmitted the first from Imam Badr al-Din al-Kardari. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:395] And Mulla Miskin said: It is a trust, except that the difference between them is that wadi'a is specific and amana is general. End. He said in al-Fath according to this view: Indeed, amana may be without a contract, so on this there is generality and specificity between them. And this difference mentioned by the commentator agrees with the second difference attributed in al-Bahr to al-Nihaya. But according to what was mentioned first in al-Bahr and attributed to al-Kardari, there is disparity between them. He is not liable etc.: i.e., because wadi'a is a trust, the depositary (with fatha) is not liable for the destruction of the deposit in his hand absolutely, whether it perishes in a way that could be guarded against or not, and whether it perishes alone without the destruction of the depositary's property or not. Similarly, he is not liable if it is stolen; because of his statement: 'There is no liability on the depositary except the treacherous'; and al-mughall is the treacherous and al-ighlal is treachery. (Fath) And for the depositary etc., because he preserves it in the same way he preserves his own property. And the meaning of 'iyal (dependents) is those who reside with him actually or legally, not those he supports financially. So included is the wife; he may give it to his husband (i.e., she may give it to her husband). Excluded is the hired worker who does not reside with him. We said 'or legally' because if he gives it to a young child and his wife, and they are in another locality, he is not liable even if he does not visit them nor spend on them, but it is required that the child be capable of preservation. Thus in al-Khulasa. And it is required that those in his household be trustworthy; because if he gives to his wife and she is not trustworthy, and he knows that, or he leaves it in his house where there are deposits of people and goes away, and it is lost, he is liable. In al-Khulasa and al-Nihaya. The apparent meaning of the texts is that the other being in his household is a condition, and this was chosen in al-Khulasa. He said: Parents are like strangers, so one must be in his household. Similarly, the author of al-Nihaya, following others, chose the non-requirement, and said: Fatwa is upon it, so he may give to his agent, or a trusted person among his trustworthy not in his household, or his partner in mufawada or 'inan. In al-Khulasa: One in his household may give to one in his household. If he forbids him from giving to some of his household and he gives, if he had no alternative to giving, he is not liable; otherwise, he is liable. If he said: Keep it in this house, and he kept it in another house of that same compound, he is not liable unless the forbidden house faced the street; then he is liable, just as if he said: Keep it in this house, and he kept it in another house; then he is liable unless the other house is like the first or more secure; then he is not liable. And it will come in full. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:395] Then if he keeps it etc.: i.e., if he keeps it with other than his dependents, he is liable. So it indicates that the depositary should not (re-)deposit. If he deposits and it perishes with the second, if the first did not separate (from it), there is no liability on either. If he separated, the first is liable according to Abu Hanifa in his view, and the second is not liable. If he deposited without permission, then the owner authorized, the first exits from the matter. Thus in al-Khulasa. And returning to the owner's dependents =
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on preservation of the deposit. It first revisits the distinction between wadi'a and amana, stating that the preferred view is that wadi'a is specific (created by contract) while amana is general (may arise without contract). The depositary is not liable for loss absolutely, whether preventable or not, and even if stolen, based on the hadith. The core ruling is that the depositary may keep the deposit himself or with his household ('iyal); 'iyal refers to those who live with him, not just those he supports. Included are wife, minor children capable of preservation; excluded are hired workers who do not live with him. If he gives it to someone not in his household, he becomes liable for loss. There is an ikhtilaf whether it is a condition that the person must be part of his household; the fatwa is on non-requirement, allowing deposit with an agent or trusted person not in household. Detailed scenarios: if he deposits with another without permission and the original depositary separates from it, he is liable; if no separation, no liability. If the owner later ratifies, the first depositary is absolved. The passage lists various edge cases regarding place of keeping.

Key Terms
عيال ('iyal): Dependents who reside with the depositary, whether actually or legally, regardless of financial support.
المودع لا يودع (al-mudi' la yudi'): The depositary should not re-deposit the item with another; if he does, he becomes liable if it perishes after separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His wife residing with him
B) His minor child living with him
C) His hired worker who does not reside with him
D) His adult son living with him

Key Takeaway
The depositary is not liable for loss unless he gives the deposit to a non-household member or to an untrustworthy household member knowing their dishonesty.

Removing Transgression in Deposit: Restoration of Trust without New Contract
Rulings on spending part of deposit and on removing transgression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that liability for transgression in a deposit ceases upon removal of the transgression, and distinguish this from the ruling for borrowers and lessees, while evaluating the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Liability from transgression ceases once the transgression is removed—trust is restored without a new contract.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a key practical issue in deposit law: can a depositary who has used the deposit without permission regain his trusteeship simply by stopping the unauthorized use? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on the principle that the depositary is commanded to preserve and, by returning to conformity, he fulfills that command. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the intra-Hanafi disagreement on spending part of the deposit, and the dispute with the Shafi'i school, which holds that trust cannot be restored without a new contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on removal of transgression
State clearly that in Hanafi fiqh, if the depositary (mudi‘) commits an act of transgression (e.g., riding the deposited animal) and then stops and returns the item to its proper state, his liability for the deposit ceases immediately. Trust is restored without a new contract.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing school's position and its proof
The Shafi'i school (followed by Maliki and Hanbali) holds that transgression voids the deposit contract. The depositary becomes a guarantor and can only be absolved by returning the deposit to the owner; a new contract is needed to restore trust. They analogize to the borrower/lessee who transgresses.
The key proof is the contradiction between liability (daman) and trust (amanah).
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the opposing proof
The Hanafi school responds by arguing that liability was imposed to remedy the harm caused by transgression. Once the transgression is removed, the harm is gone, so liability ends. The deposit contract remains valid because the depositary is still capable of safekeeping. The analogy with the hired guard who neglects temporarily but resumes his duty supports this.
The Hanafi argue that the borrower/lessee is different because their possession is for their own benefit, not for preservation.
4
Distinguish the depositary from the borrower/lessee
Contrast the depositary, whose liability ceases upon removal of transgression, with the borrower (musta‘ir) and lessee (musta’jir), who do not regain trust by merely ceasing the transgression. They must return the property to the owner's hand because their possession is for self-benefit, not for safekeeping.
Zufar disagreed on this point, holding that removal of transgression also absolves them.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider a depositary who rides the deposited horse without permission, then returns it to the stable. According to Hanafi, if the horse later dies naturally, he is not liable. According to Shafi‘i, he remains liable unless the horse is returned to the owner personally.
This thamarah al-khilaf shows the practical impact of the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوديعة
التعدي في الوديعة
ولو أنفق بعضها فردّ مثله فخلطه بالباقي ضمن الكل، وإن تعدّى فيها ثم أزال التعدي، زال الضمان بخلاف المستعير والمستأجر . ولو أنفق إلخ: أي ولو أنفق المودع بعض الوديعة، ثم رد مثل ما أنفق، فخلطه بالباقي ضمن الكل؛ لأنه ضمن البعض بالإنفاق، وصار ضامناً للبعض الآخر؛ لكونه خلط ماله بها، وعند الثلاثة يضمن ما أنفق فقط، وعن مالك لا يضمن ما أنفق أيضاً. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٢/٢] ضمن الكل: أي البعض بالإنفاق، والبعض بالخلط؛ لأنه متعد بالإنفاق منها ورد مثله باق على ملكه، وقد خلطه بما بقي من الوديعة فضمن الجميع، والمراد بالخلط هنا خلط لا يتميز معه أما لو جعل على ماله علامة خلطه بها حيث يتأتى التمييز لا يضمن إلا ما أنفق كذا في "الخلاصة : وقيد بقوله: "فرد مثلها"؛ لأنه لو لم يرد كان ضامناً لما أنفق خاصة؛ لأنه حافظ للباقي، و لم يتعيب؛ لأنه مما لا يضره التبعيض؛ لأن الكلام فيما إذا كانت الوديعة دراهم أو دنانير أو أشياء من المكيل والموزون فهو كما لو أودعه وديعتين، فأنفق إحداهما، لا يكون ضامنا للأخرى، كذا في "النهاية". وإن تعدى إلخ: أي وإن تعدى المودع فيها بأن كانت الوديعة دابة فركبها، أو ثوبا فلبسه، أو عبداً فاستخدمه، أو أودعها غيره، ثم أزال التعدي ورد إلى يده على ما كان زال الضمان الواجب بالتعدي. (مسكين) وفي "الفتح": قوله: زال الضمان؛ لأنه مأمور بالحفظ في كل الأوقات، فإذا خالف في البعض، ثم رجع أتى بالمأمور به كما إذا استأجره للحفظ شهراً، فترك الحفظ في بعضه، ثم حفظ الباقي استحق الأجر بقدره. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٩/٧] ثم ذكر أن الضمان إنما يزول بشرط أنه لا يغرم على العود إلى التعدي، حتى لو نزع ثوب الوديعة ليلاً، ومن عزمه أن يلبسه نهاراً، ثم سرق ليلاً، لا يبرأ عن الضمان معزيا للظهيرية قال العلامة الحموي: ومعنى زال الضمان أنه كانت الوديعة بحيث لو هلكت ضمنت فزال هذا المعنى. (فتح)
ثم أزال التعدي إلخ: يعني أزال المودع التعدي وصدقه المالك، فإن كذبه لا يبرأ عن الضمان إلا أن يقيم البينة، كذا في "الحموي" عن "العمادي". (فتح) زال الضمان: هذا عندنا وقال الشافعي لا يبرأ عن الضمان؛ لأن عقد الوديعة قد ارتفع حين صار ضامناً؛ للمنافاة بين الضمان والأمانة، فلا يبرأ إلا بالرد على صاحبها، ولا تعود الأمانة إلا بعقد جديد، فصار كالمستعير والمستأجر وكالجحود، وبقوله قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا: أن الضمان وجب رفعاً للضرر الواقع، وقد ارتفع بالعود إلى الوفاق، فلا يضمن. (فتح عيني) بخلاف المستعير والمستأجر : [ أي لا يزول ضمان المستعير والمستأجر إذا تعدا ثم أزالا التعدي] يعني إذا تعدى في المستعار والمستأجر بأن استعار ثوبا ليلبسه يوما، فلبسه يومين ونزعه للتسليم، أو استأجر الدابة ليركبها أياماً معدودة، أو ليحمل عليها أمناء معلومة، فركبها أو حملها أكثر منها، ثم ردها كما كانت، لم يبرأ، خلافاً لزفر الله فيهما؛ لأن البراءة منه إنما تكون بإعادة يد المالك حقيقة أو حكماً، ولم يوجد ذلك؛ لأن قبضهما لأنفسهما -
Book of Deposit. Transgression in the Deposit. And if he spends some of it, then returns its like and mixes it with the remainder, he becomes liable for the whole. And if he transgresses in it, then removes the transgression, the liability ceases, unlike the borrower (musta‘ir) and the lessee (musta’jir). 'And if he spends' etc.: i.e., if the depositary spends some of the deposit, then returns the like of what he spent, and mixes it with the remainder, he becomes liable for the whole, because he became liable for the part by spending, and becomes liable for the other part because he mixed his property with it. According to the Three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar), he is liable only for what he spent. From Malik, he is not liable for what he spent either. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/252] 'Becomes liable for the whole': i.e., for the part by spending, and for the rest by mixing, because he transgressed by spending from it, and his returning its like remains his property, and he mixed it with what remained of the deposit, so he becomes liable for all. What is meant by mixing here is mixing that does not allow distinction. But if he placed a mark on his property such that it can be distinguished, he is liable only for what he spent. As in al-Khulasa. And he restricted it with his saying 'returns its like' because if he did not return, he would be liable only for what he spent, because he is a preserver of the remainder and did not transgress with it because it is not harmed by partial loss; because the discussion is when the deposit is dirhams, dinars, or things that are measured or weighed, so it is like if he deposited two deposits and spent one, he is not liable for the other. As in al-Nihaya. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/399] (then) 'And if he transgresses' etc.: i.e., if the depositary transgresses in it, such as if the deposit was an animal and he rode it, or a garment and he wore it, or a slave and he employed him, or he gave it to another as a deposit, then he removes the transgression and returns it to his hand as it was, the liability required by the transgression ceases. (Miskin) And in al-Fath: His saying: the liability ceases, because he is commanded to preserve at all times. So if he violated in some part, then returned, he does what is commanded, like if he hired him for preservation for a month, and he left off preservation in some of it, then preserved the remainder, he deserves the wage proportionally. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/399] Then he mentioned that liability ceases only on condition that he does not incur a cost for returning to transgression. So if he removed the deposit garment at night with the intention of wearing it in the day, then it was stolen at night, he is not absolved from liability, referencing al-Zahiriya. Al-Hamawi said: The meaning of 'liability ceases' is that the deposit was such that if it had been destroyed, he would have been liable, so this meaning ceased. (Fath) 'Then he removes the transgression' etc.: i.e., the depositary removes the transgression and the owner confirms him. If he denies him, he is not absolved from liability unless he brings evidence. As in al-Hamawi from al-‘Imadi. (Fath) 'The liability ceases': This is our view. And al-Shafi‘i said: He is not absolved from liability, because the deposit contract is voided when he became liable, due to the contradiction between liability and trust. So he is not absolved except by returning to its owner, and trust does not return except with a new contract. So it is like the borrower and lessee, and like the denier. And Malik and Ahmad agreed with him. Our proof: that liability was incurred to remove the harm that occurred, and it has been removed by returning to conformity, so he is not liable. (Fath ‘Ayni) Contrary to the borrower and lessee: [i.e., the liability of the borrower and lessee does not cease if they transgress and then remove the transgression]. Meaning, if one transgresses in the borrowed or leased item, such as borrowing a garment to wear for one day and he wears it for two days and removes it for return, or leases an animal to ride for a certain number of days or to carry known loads, and he rides it or carries more than that, then returns it as it was, he is not absolved, contrary to Zufar in both, because absolution from it is only by restoring the owner's hand actually or legally, and that did not occur because their possession is for themselves.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with two scenarios of transgression (ta‘addi) in a deposit and the removal of liability. The first scenario: the depositary spends part of the deposit (e.g., takes some money from it) and then replaces it with an equal amount, mixing it with the remainder. According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa), he becomes liable for the entire deposit (the spent portion and the rest) because he committed transgression by spending (liability for that part) and by mixing his own property with the deposit (liability for the rest). The 'Three' (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) hold he is liable only for what he spent. If he marks his property so it can be distinguished, he is liable only for the spent part. The second scenario: the depositary commits a transgression (e.g., uses the deposit unlawfully) but then removes the transgression (e.g., stops using and returns it to the proper state). In Hanafi law, the liability that arose due to transgression ceases once the transgression is removed, provided the depositary has not incurred a new cost or intention to repeat the transgression. The analogy given is with a hired guard who neglects his duty temporarily but then resumes; he still receives proportional pay. The deposit contract remains valid, and trust is restored. This is the Hanafi position. Al-Shafi‘i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that once liability arises from transgression, the deposit contract is voided, and the depositary becomes a guarantor (damin); subsequently, he can only be freed by returning the deposit to the owner, and trust cannot be restored without a new contract. The Hanafi argument is that liability was imposed to compensate for the harm, and once the transgression is undone, the harm is removed, so liability ceases. This is contrasted with borrowed (musta‘ir) or leased (musta’jir) property: if the borrower or lessee transgresses (e.g., exceeds usage), they do not regain trust by simply ceasing; they must return the property to the owner's hand. The text notes that Zufar disagreed on this point within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
التعدي (al-ta‘addi): Transgression; unauthorized use or handling of the deposit that violates the depositary's duty of preservation.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage arising from transgression.
المستعير (al-musta‘ir): The borrower; one who receives property for free use with the owner's permission.
المستأجر (al-musta’jir): The lessee; one who rents property for use in exchange for payment.
الثلاثة (al-thalatha): The three: Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar – disciples of Abu Hanifa who sometimes held different opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if the depositary (المودع) transgresses by riding the deposited animal without permission, then stops and returns it to the stable, what happens to his liability for the animal?
A) He remains liable until he returns it to the owner personally.
B) His liability ceases immediately once he removes the transgression.
C) He is never liable because riding is not considered transgression for a depositary.
D) He becomes co-owner of the animal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a depositary who transgresses can restore the trust and cease liability simply by removing the transgression, unlike borrowers and lessees who must return the property to the owner's hand.

Liability for Mixing Equivalent Goods with a Deposit
Ruling on mixing similar goods with deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a depositary spends part of a deposit and returns a similar amount, and apply the distinction between indistinguishable mixing and marked mixing.

Core Idea
Returning an equivalent after spending does not erase liability; mixing it indistinguishably makes the depositary liable for the entire deposit.

This passage presents a nuanced scenario in the law of deposit (wadiʿah): what happens when a depositary spends part of the deposit, then replenishes with an equivalent amount and mixes it with the remainder? The ruling hinges on whether the mixing is distinguishable or not. Understanding this helps clarify the Hanafi concept of taʿaddī (transgression) and its consequences, as well as the limits of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The depositary spends part of the deposit, then returns a similar amount and mixes it with the remainder. The Hanafi ruling in the matn: he is liable for the entire deposit.
The ruling is from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, supported by the commentary.
2
Understand the two-stage liability
Liability arises first for the spent part due to taʿaddī (unauthorized use). Then, by mixing his own property (the replacement) with the remaining deposit indistinguishably, he becomes liable for that part as well, because the owner cannot separate his property.
The key is that the mixing is inseparable (khalṭ lā yutamayyaz).
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds full liability for the entire deposit. The 'three' (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar) hold that he is liable only for what he spent. Imam Malik holds no liability at all.
The matn and commentary uphold Abu Hanifa's view as the mufta bihi.
4
Apply the condition of indistinguishability
If the depositary marks his replacement property so that it can be distinguished, he is liable only for what he spent. The ruling applies only to fungible goods (mithliyyāt) like dirhams, dinars, or measured/weighed items.
Restriction: 'فردّ مثلها' (returned its equivalent) – if he did not return, he is liable only for the spent part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أنفق بعضها فردّ مثله فخلطه بالباقي ضمن الكل. ولو أنفق إلخ: أي ولو أنفق المودع بعض الوديعة، ثم رد مثل ما أنفق، فخلطه بالباقي ضمن الكل؛ لأنه ضمن البعض بالإنفاق، وصار ضامناً للبعض الآخر؛ لكونه خلط ماله بها، وعند الثلاثة يضمن ما أنفق فقط، وعن مالك لا يضمن ما أنفق أيضاً. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٢/٢] ضمن الكل: أي البعض بالإنفاق، والبعض بالخلط؛ لأنه متعد بالإنفاق منها ورد مثله باق على ملكه، وقد خلطه بما بقي من الوديعة فضمن الجميع، والمراد بالخلط هنا خلط لا يتميز معه أما لو جعل على ماله علامة خلطه بها حيث يتأتى التمييز لا يضمن إلا ما أنفق كذا في "الخلاصة" : وقيد بقوله: "فرد مثلها"؛ لأنه لو لم يرد كان ضامناً لما أنفق خاصة؛ لأنه حافظ للباقي، و لم يتعيب؛ لأنه مما لا يضره التبعيض؛ لأن الكلام فيما إذا كانت الوديعة دراهم أو دنانير أو أشياء من المكيل ،والموزون فهو كما لو أودعه وديعتين، فأنفق إحداهما، لا يكون ضامنا للأخرى، كذا في "النهاية". [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٩/٧]
And if he spent part of it, then returned its equivalent, and mixed it with the remainder, he is liable for all of it. Explanation: If the depositary spent part of the deposit, then returned a similar amount, and mixed it with the remainder, he is liable for all; because he became liable for the part by spending, and becomes liable for the other part because he mixed his own property with it. According to the three, he is liable only for what he spent; according to Malik, he is not liable for what he spent either. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/252] 'Liable for all' means he is liable for the part by spending and for the part by mixing; because he transgressed by spending from it, and the replacement he returned remains his property, and he mixed it with what remained of the deposit, so he is liable for all. The intended mixing here is inseparable mixing; if he placed a mark on his property by which it can be distinguished, he is only liable for what he spent, as stated in al-Khulasa. And he restricted it to 'returned its equivalent' because if he did not return, he would be liable only for what he spent, as he is a guardian of the remainder and it is not defective since fractional division does not harm it; the discussion is when the deposit is dirhams, dinars, or measured/weighed items, so it is like if he deposited two deposits with him and spent one, he is not liable for the other, as in al-Nihaya. [Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/399]
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and explanation. It addresses the scenario where a depositary (al-mūdiʿ) spends part of the deposit and then returns a similar amount, mixing it with the remainder. The Hanafi ruling (according to the matn) is that he becomes liable for the entire deposit. The reasoning is that he first became liable for the spent portion by his transgression (taʿaddī) of spending, and then by mixing his own replacement property with the remaining deposit, he also becomes liable for that part. However, there is an intra-Hanafi difference: 'the three' (likely Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar) hold that he is only liable for what he spent. Malik holds he is not liable at all. The commentary clarifies that the mixing must be indistinguishable—if he marks his property so it can be separated, he is only liable for what he spent. The ruling applies only to fungible goods (mithliyyāt) like dirhams, dinars, and measured/weighed items, where partial use does not harm the rest. If he had two separate deposits and spent one, he is not liable for the other.

Key Terms
التعدي (al-taʿaddī): Transgression or unauthorized use of the deposit by the depositary, such as spending or using without permission.
الخلط (al-khalṭ): Mixing the depositary's own property (the replacement) with the remaining deposit. If indistinguishable, it creates liability for the whole.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage; here, the obligation to replace the deposit if it is lost or mixed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if a depositary spends part of a deposit, then returns a similar amount and mixes it with the remainder indistinguishably?
A) He is liable only for the part he spent
B) He is liable for the entire deposit
C) He is not liable for anything
D) He is liable only for the part he mixed

Key Takeaway
Indistinguishable mixing of a returned equivalent with a deposit after spending creates liability for the entire deposit, even though the total quantity is restored.

Six Conditions for Valid Juhūd of a Depositary
Conditions for effective juhūd of depositary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six conditions under which a depositary's denial (juhūd) dissolves the contract of deposit, and apply them to determine whether a post-denial confession removes liability.

Core Idea
A depositary's denial, if it meets all conditions, dissolves the trust contract—confession alone cannot restore it.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the critical distinction between a depositary who denies the deposit under specific conditions and one who denies without them. In Hanafi fiqh, a valid denial (juhūd) that meets six conditions actually rescinds the amāna contract, so a subsequent confession does not automatically restore the trust. Understanding these conditions is essential for determining when the depositary remains liable even after confessing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The passage states that if a depositary denies the deposit under specific conditions and later confesses, liability does not cease because the denial rescinded the contract. The contract cannot be revived except by a new contract, like the denial of an agent or a party in a sale.
Note the analogy to wakāla and sale—juhūd in each case terminates the contract.
2
List the first condition: denial of the deposit itself
The depositary must deny the deposit (e.g., 'I never received it'). If he instead claims the owner gave it as a gift or sold it, that is not considered juhūd; if it perishes, he is not liable.
This distinguishes juhūd from other claims that do not breach the trust.
3
List the second condition: denial after demand for return
The denial must occur after the owner demands the deposit back. If the owner casually asks 'How is my deposit?' and the depositary denies, there is no liability because the denial was not in response to a demand for return.
The owner's intent matters—demand for return triggers the trust's dissolution.
4
List the third to sixth conditions
The remaining conditions: (3) the depositary must move the deposit from its place during denial; (4) the deposit must be movable (not real estate); (5) there must be no external threat (e.g., enemy) that would justify denial as protection; (6) the depositary must not produce the deposit before confessing. If he produces it and the owner leaves it, it becomes a new deposit.
Each condition has a specific rationale; absence prevents liability.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a scenario, check each condition. If all are met, the depositary remains liable after confession; if even one is missing, confession may restore trust or no liability existed.
Practice this for mastery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإقراره بعد جحوده. يعني أن المودع إذا جحد الوديعة بأن قال: لم يودعني مالكها بعد طلب ردها ونقلها من مكانها وقت الإنكار، وكانت منقولا، ولم يكن هناك من يخاف منه عليها، ولم يحضرها بعد الجحود لمالكها، ثم أقر بها لا يزول الضمان؛ لأن الجحود رفع للعقد، فيفسخ به العقد، فلا يعود إلا بعقد جديد كجحود الوكيل وجحود أحد المتعاقدين البيع. عن الوكالة، قيدنا بكونه أنكر الإيداع؛ لأن المودع لو ادعى أن المالك وهبها منه . أو باعها له وأنكر صاحبها، ثم هلكت لا ضمان على المودع، كذا في "الخلاصة"، وقيدنا بكونه بعد الطلب؛ لأنه لو قال له: "ما حال وديعتي عندك؟" ليشكر على حفظها، فجحدها لا ضمان عليه وقيدنا بكونه نقلها؛ لأنه لو لم ينقلها من مكانها حال جحوده، فهلكت لا ضمان عليه، وقيدنا بكونه منقولا؛ لأنها لو كانت عقارا لا يضمن بالجحود عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد في الأصح، ذكره الشارح في الغصب وقيدنا بكونه لم يكن من يخاف عليها منه؛ لأنه لو جحدها في وجه عدو، ويخاف عليها التلف إن أقر ثم هلكت لا يضمنها؛ لأنه إنما أراد حفظها، وقيدنا بكونه لم يحضرها؛ لأنه لو جحدها ثم أحضرها، فقال له صاحبها: دعها وديعة عندك فهلكت، فإن أمكنه أخذها فلم يأخذها لم يضمن؛ لأنه إيداع جديد، وإن لم يمكن ضمن؛ لأنه لم يتم الرد. [البحر الرائق: ٤٠٠/٧]
And his confession after his denial. That is, if the depositary denies the deposit by saying: 'The owner did not deposit it with me' after demand for its return and moving it from its place at the time of denial, and it was movable, and there was no one to fear for it from, and he did not produce it after denial to its owner, then he confessed to it, liability does not cease because the denial dissolved the contract, so the contract is rescinded by it, and it does not return except with a new contract, like the denial of the agent and the denial of one of the two contracting parties in a sale. Regarding agency, we restricted that he denied the deposit itself; because if the depositary claimed that the owner gifted it to him or sold it to him and he denied the owner, then it perishes, there is no liability on the depositary, as in al-Khulasa. And we restricted that he denied after demand; because if he said to him: 'What is the status of my deposit with you?' to thank him for guarding it, and he denied, there is no liability. And we restricted that he moved it; because if he did not move it from its place during his denial, and it perishes, there is no liability. And we restricted that it is movable; because if it were real estate, he is not liable by denial according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad in the more sound view, as the commentator mentioned in Ghash. And we restricted that there is no one to fear for it from; because if he denied it in the face of an enemy and fears its destruction if he admits, then it perishes, he is not liable because he only intended to protect it. And we restricted that he did not produce it; because if he denied it then produced it, and its owner said: 'Leave it as a deposit with you', and it perishes, if it was possible for him to take it and he did not, he is not liable because it is a new deposit; if it was not possible, he is liable because the return was not completed. [Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/400]
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling on a depositary who denies the deposit and later confesses. The general principle is that the denial, under specific conditions, nullifies the contract of deposit, so a subsequent confession does not restore the trust; the depositary remains liable for the deposit unless it is physically returned. However, this effect only occurs if all the following conditions are met: (1) The denial is of the deposit itself—if the depositary claims the owner gave it to him as a gift or sold it, that is not considered a denial that creates liability. (2) The denial occurs after the owner demands its return—if the owner asks casually, e.g., 'How is my deposit?' to thank him, denial does not create liability. (3) The depositary moves the deposit from its original location at the time of denial—if he keeps it in place, he is not liable. (4) The deposit is movable property—for real estate, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, denial does not create liability, though Muhammad holds the opposite. (5) There is no external threat from which the depositary seeks to protect the deposit by denying—e.g., an enemy present; in that case, denial is considered protection and does not create liability. (6) The depositary does not produce the deposit before confessing—if he produces it and the owner leaves it with him again, that is a new deposit; if the owner could take it but doesn't, no liability; if he can't take it, liability remains.

Key Terms
الجحود (al-juḥūd): Denial by the depositary that he received the deposit; under specific conditions, this dissolves the trust contract.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Confession or admission after denial; does not automatically restore the trust or remove liability.
الرد (al-radd): Return of the deposit to its owner. Physical return is required to end liability after a valid juhūd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for a depositary's denial (juhūd) to create liability that persists despite later confession?
A) The denial occurs after the owner demands the deposit
B) The depositary moves the deposit from its place
C) The deposit is immovable property (real estate)
D) The depositary does not produce the deposit before confessing

Key Takeaway
A depositary's valid denial rescinds the trust contract; therefore, a later confession does not remove liability unless the deposit is physically returned.
