Traveling with the Deposit: Intra-Hanafi and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on traveling with deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, al-Shafi'i, and Malik on traveling with a deposit, and explain the conditions under which it is permissible or impermissible.

Core Idea
A depositary may travel with the deposit if no prohibition and no danger, but Hanafi scholars differ on distance and costs.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two principal students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the depositary's right to travel with the deposit. It also contrasts the Hanafi position with that of the Shafi'i and Maliki schools, which absolutely forbid such travel. By studying this passage, you will see how the Hanafis balance the duty of preservation with practical realities, and how different conditions affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the initial ruling
The passage opens by stating the basic rule: the depositary may travel with the deposit (وله أن يسافر بها). Note the immediate conditions: no prohibition from the owner and no fear for the deposit. This is the foundation of the discussion.
This is the default position according to Abu Hanifa, as later clarified.
2
Trace the intra-Hanafi differences
The text then presents the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, who restrict Abu Hanifa's absolute permission. Abu Yusuf allows only short distances; Muhammad prohibits travel for deposits that have weight or expense. Understand their reasons: the cost of return (مؤنة الرد) and the increased risk on long journeys.
These restrictions are based on practical considerations, not on a different interpretation of the foundational duty.
3
Analyze the inter-madhhab objection and Hanafi response
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools argue that traveling exposes the deposit to destruction (عرض المال على الهلاك). The passage then provides Abu Hanifa's counter-proof: the depositary is commanded to preserve absolutely, and traveling can be a form of preservation; the expenses are necessary.
The Hanafi response emphasizes that the means of preservation are not limited to staying in one place.
4
Examine the additional conditions mentioned after the dispute
The passage concludes with two conditional scenarios: (1) if the road is frightening, travel is not allowed if there is an alternative; (2) if the owner explicitly forbids leaving the city, the depositary is liable if he leaves and has an alternative. These conditions refine the ruling.
These conditions align more closely with the restrictive views of the Sahibayn, suggesting the mufta bihi may be the cautious approach.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa's view, a depositary can travel with the deposit without liability as long as no prohibition and no extraordinary danger. Under the Sahibayn's views, he must assess distance and weight. Under Shafi'i/Maliki, he cannot travel at all and would be liable for any loss.
This affects the liability of the depositary if the deposit is lost during travel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله أن يسافر بها. أي وللمودع أن يسافر بها أي بالوديعة عند عدم النهي من صاحب الوديعة، وعند عدم الخوف عليها بالإخراج، وهذا على الإطلاق قول أبي حنيفة، وقال أبو يوسف له الخروج بها إلى مسافة قصيرة، وإن طالت لا يخرج فيما له حمل ومؤنة؛ لأنه يلزمه مؤنة الرد، ولأن القصيرة لا يخاف فيها عادة، وقال محمد الله: لا يخرج بما له حمل ومؤنة وقال الشافعي: ليس له أن يخرج مطلقاً، بها وبه قال مالك؛ لأنه عرض المال على الهلاك؛ لأن المفازة مهلكة، ولأبي حنيفة له: أنه أمره بالحفظ مطلقاً، وقد أتى به، وما ذكره من المؤنة من ضرورات حفظ ماله، فلا يأباه، ولو كان الطريق مخيفاً ليس له أن يسافر بها إن كان له منه بد، وإن لم يكن له منه بد وسافر مع أهله لا يضمن ولو نهاه أن يخرج بها من المصر، فخرج بها ضمن إن كان له منه بد، وإن لم يكن
And he may travel with it. That is, the depositary may travel with the deposit, i.e., the deposit, on condition of no prohibition from the deposit owner and on condition of no fear for it from being taken out. This is, absolutely, the statement of Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: He may take it out to a short distance; if the distance is long, he may not take out what has weight and expense, because the expense of return becomes incumbent on him, and because a short distance is usually not feared. Muhammad said: He may not take out what has weight and expense. Al-Shafi'i said: He may not take it out absolutely, and Malik said the same, because he exposes the property to destruction, as the desert is destructive. Abu Hanifa's proof is that he commanded him to preserve absolutely, and he did so; and what he mentioned of expense is a necessity of preserving his property, so it is not objectionable. And if the road is frightening, he may not travel with it if there is an alternative for him; if there is no alternative and he travels with his family, he is not liable. And if he forbade him to take it out of the city, and he took it out, he is liable if there is an alternative for him; if not, ...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a depositary may travel with the deposit. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa allows it unconditionally (as long as the owner has not prohibited it and there is no fear of loss). Abu Yusuf allows only short distances; for long journeys, if the deposit is heavy or requires expense, he may not because the cost of return falls on him, and long journeys typically pose more risk. Muhammad prohibits traveling with any deposit that has weight and expense. The commentary also mentions the inter-madhhab dispute: al-Shafi'i and Malik absolutely forbid traveling with the deposit, arguing that it exposes the property to destruction in dangerous wilderness. The Hanafi response (for Abu Hanifa) is that the depositary is commanded to preserve the deposit absolutely, and traveling can be a means of preservation; the expenses incurred are part of preserving the owner's property, so they are acceptable. Additional conditions: if the route is dangerous, the depositary may not travel if he has an alternative (e.g., a safe route). If there is no alternative and he travels with his family (ensuring protection), he is not liable. If the owner explicitly forbids leaving the city, and the depositary does so, he becomes liable if he had an alternative route or some way to avoid it.

Key Terms
السفر بالوديعة (al-safar bi al-wadīʿa): Traveling with the deposit; the act of a depositary taking the deposit on a journey.
مؤنة الرد (muʾnat al-radd): The expense of returning the deposit to its owner or location; a factor in restricting travel.
الخوف (al-khawf): Fear for the safety of the deposit, e.g., due to dangerous roads or enemies. If present, travel may be prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when may a depositary travel with the deposit?
A) Only if the owner explicitly permits
B) Absolutely, as long as there is no prohibition and no fear
C) Only for short distances
D) Never, because it violates the trust

Key Takeaway
A depositary may travel with the deposit if the owner has not prohibited it and there is no special danger, but within the Hanafi school, the more cautious views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad often prevail in practice.

Traveling with a Deposit: Conditions and Liability
Ruling with proof via consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depository may travel with a deposit and identify when liability is imposed, including the consensus rule for sea travel.

Core Idea
A depository is not liable unless he violates conditions or travels by sea, where danger is prevalent.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition addresses a common scenario: a person entrusted with a deposit who wishes to travel. The ruling balances the rights of the depositor and the practical needs of the depository. We will explore the two key conditions—owner's permission and road safety—and the special rule for sea travel, supported by consensus and later scholarly analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Base Ruling
The act is the depository traveling with the deposit. The base ruling is permissibility (mubah) when two conditions are met: the owner has not prohibited it, and the route is safe.
2
Locate the Dalil and Its Type
The passage cites a consensus (ijma) that traveling by sea imposes liability. The dalil is a statement of consensus, which is a strong proof in Hanafi usul.
Consensus is considered a primary source of law.
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
The phrase 'they agreed' indicates unanimous agreement. The rationale (wajh) is that sea travel involves prevalent danger, making it akin to a violation of trust.
This is a qati' dalalah even though thubut is zanni.
4
Distinguish Special Cases
The passage distinguishes the father/guardian traveling with child's wealth (no liability) and the agent for sale: liability depends on whether the location was restricted.
These are exceptions based on inherent authority or specific authorization.
5
Evaluate the Objection and Response
Al-Maqdisi objects that the rule on sea travel contradicts the permissibility of sea travel for Hajj when safety is expected. The response is that the restriction here is based on the rationale of prevalent danger.
This shows how later scholars refined the ruling through critical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عند عدم النهي والخوف ولو أودعا شيئا لم يدفع المودع إلى أحدهما حظه حتى يحضر الآخر، أي رجلان أحد الرجلين الرجل الآخر عن عند قالت له منه بد لا يضمن، وأجمعوا على أنه لو سافر بها في البحر يضمن قاله الإسبيجابي ليه، وقال قاضي خان في "شرح الجامع الصغير": وأجمعوا على أن الأب والوصي إذا سافر بمال الابن أو اليتيم لا يضمن، والوكيل بالبيع إذا سافر بما وكله بالبيع، قالوا: إن قيده بمكان بأن قال له بعه بالكوفة، فسافر به يضمن، وإن أطلق إطلاقاً، فسافر به لا يضمن إذا سرق أوضاع فيما لا حمل ،له ويضمن فيما له حمل ومؤونة. (عيني) عند عدم النهي : أي فهي المالك عن المسافرة بالوديعة. (ملا مسكين عيني والخوف: أي له أن يسافر بها إذا لم يكن الطريق مخوفاً، ومن المخوف السفر بها في البحر ؛ لأن الغالب فيه العطب، كذا في "البحر" [٤٠١/٧] "الاختيار" وتعقبه الحموي نقلاً عن المقدسي، فقال: يخالف هذا الإطلاق قولهم في الحج: يجب إذا كان الغالب السلامة، ولو بحراً، انتهى، وأجيب بأن التقييد مستفاد . من تعليله. (فتح)
In the absence of prohibition and fear, and if two men deposit something, the depository does not give to one his share until the other comes, meaning two men, one of the two men, the other man, from when she said, he has no alternative to it, so he is not liable, and they agreed that if he travels with it by sea, he is liable, said by al-Ispijabi, and Qadi Khan said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: They agreed that the father and guardian, if they travel with the child's or orphan's wealth, are not liable, and the agent for sale, if he travels with what he is authorized to sell, they said: If he restricted it to a place by saying 'sell it in Kufa,' then he travels with it, he is liable, and if he made it absolutely unrestricted, then if he travels with it, he is not liable if it is stolen or lost in something that has no carrying cost, and he is liable in something that has carrying cost and provision. ('Ayni) 'In the absence of prohibition' means the owner allows traveling with the deposit. (Mulla Miskin 'Ayni) 'And fear' means he may travel with it if the road is not fearful, and among fearful travel is traveling with it by sea, because destruction is prevalent, as in al-Bahr [7:401] and al-Ikhtiyar, and al-Hamawi objected by quoting al-Maqdisi, saying: This contradicts their statement in Hajj: it is obligatory if safety is prevalent, even by sea, and it was answered that the restriction is derived from its rationale. (Fath)
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layer commentary on the matn concerning travelling with a deposit. The core ruling is that a depository (mūda‘) may travel with the deposit only if two conditions are met: the owner has not prohibited it, and the route is not dangerous. If the depository travels with the deposit in a way that violates these conditions, such as by sea (which is inherently dangerous due to frequent shipwrecks), he becomes liable for loss or damage. The passage cites a consensus (ijmā‘) that sea travel with the deposit imposes liability. It then distinguishes between different trustees: a father or guardian travelling with a child's or orphan's wealth is not liable, even without permission, because their authority is inherent. An agent for sale, if restricted to a specific location, is liable if he travels elsewhere; if unrestricted, he is not liable as long as the deposit is not heavy. The commentator ‘Aynī clarifies that 'absence of prohibition' means the owner's implied consent to travel. Mulla Miskin explains 'fear' as the road being unsafe, and notes that sea travel is always considered fearful. A later objection by al-Maqdisi points out an apparent contradiction with the rule for Hajj, where sea travel is permitted if safety is expected, but the response is that the restriction for deposits is based on the rationale of prevalent danger.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadī‘ah): A deposit: property entrusted by one person to another for safekeeping without compensation.
المودع (al-mūda‘): The depository: the person who receives the deposit for safekeeping.
المالك (al-mālik): The owner of the deposited property, also called the depositor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A depository is always liable if he travels with the deposit.
B) A depository is liable if he travels by sea, regardless of conditions.
C) A father traveling with his child's wealth is liable for any loss.
D) An agent with unrestricted authority is liable if he travels with light goods.

Key Takeaway
A depository may travel with a deposit only if the owner permits and the route is safe; sea travel always imposes liability due to prevalent danger.

Joint Deposit: Delivering a Share to One Depositor
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delivering a share of a joint deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on delivering a share of a joint deposit, identify the scope of the dispute (fungibles only), and explain the practical difference in liability.

Core Idea
For fungible joint deposits, Abu Hanifa forbids giving a share to one depositor alone; the Sahibayn allow it.

When two people deposit fungible goods like wheat with a depository, what happens if only one comes to claim his share? This lesson unpacks a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples on whether the depository may deliver a partial share and whether he becomes liable for the remainder.

Guided Path
1
State the case
Understand the scenario: two depositors entrust a fungible item (makīl or mawzūn) to a depository. One depositor arrives and demands his share.
The deposit is undivided.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the depository must not deliver until both are present. If he does deliver and the remainder is lost, he is liable for half its value.
This is based on the indivisibility of the trust.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the depository may deliver the share because the depositor is claiming his own property, just as if both were present. No liability attaches.
They apply istihsan overriding qiyas.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
The key practical difference is the depository's liability if the remaining deposit is lost after delivering one share. Also, the delivery itself: does it constitute a valid division? According to Abu Hanifa, it is not a valid division.
5
Note the scope of the dispute
The dispute is limited to fungible goods (dhawāt al-amthāl). For non-fungibles like clothes or animals, there is consensus that the depository must not deliver to a single depositor.
This is explicitly stated in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اودعا إلخ: أي ولو أودع رجلان شيئا عند رجل مما يكال أو يوزن، فحضر أحدهما يطلب نصيبه، لم يدفع إليه، وهو معنى قوله : لم يدفع المودع بفتح الدال إلى أحدهما أي أحد الاثنين حظه أي نصيبه حتى يحضر الآخر - أبي حنيفة لله، وهو مروي عن علي له، وقالا : له ذلك؛ لأنه طلب نصيبه كما لو حضرا، وبه الثلاثة، وإن كانت الوديعة من غير ذوات الأمثال ليس له ذلك بالإجماع رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٣/٢، ٢٥٤] لم يدفع المودع إلخ فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يجوز له الدفع، حتى لو خاصمه إلى القاضي لم يأمره بدفع نصيبه إليه في قول أبي حنيفة، وإلى أنه لو دفع إليه لا يكون قسمة، حتى إذا هلك الباقي رجع صاحبه على الآخذ، وإلى أن لأحدهما أن يأخذ حصته منها إذا ظفر بها، وإلى أنه لو دفع وارتكب الممنوع لا يضمن. وفي "فتاوى قاضي خان ما يفيده ولفظه: ثلاثة أودعوا رجلا، وقالوا لا تدفع المال إلى أحد منا حتى نجتمع، فدفع نصيب أحدهم، قال محمد: في القياس يضمن، وبه قال أبو حنيفة، وفي الاستحسان لا يضمن، وهو قول أبي يوسف، فقد جعل عدم الضمان هو الاستحسان، فكان هو المختار ، كذا في "البحر" [٤٠٢/٧] وتعقبه المقدسي فقال: كيف يكون هو المختار مع أن سائر المتون على قول الإمام، وقال الشيخ قاسم: اختار النسفي قول الإمام والمحبوبي وصدر الشريعة، وقال المقدسي: وقول بعضهم عدم الضمان هو المختار مستدلا بكون الاستحسان مخالفا لما عليه الأئمة الأعيان، بل غالب المتون عليه ،متفقون كذا في "الحموي". (فتح المعين) يحضر الآخر إلخ عند أبي حنيفة، ولو فعل ضمن نصفه وعندهما يدفع إليه نصيبه، ولا يضمن، والخلاف في المكيل والموزون، وفي "الذخيرة" ذكر الخلاف في الثياب والدواب أيضاً، وكذا في "الكافي"، والصحيح أن الخلاف فيما هو من ذوات الأمثال كالمكيلات والموزونات وفيما عداهما من الثياب والدواب والعبيد، فليس للحاضر أن يأخذ نصيبه بالإجماع. (ملا مسكين)
And if two men deposit something with a man of what is measured or weighed, then one of them comes asking for his share, he does not give it to him, this is the meaning of his statement: the depository does not give to one of the two his share until the other comes - Abu Hanifa, and it is narrated from Ali, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he has that right; because he asked for his share just as if both had come, and with him are the three, and if the deposit is not of fungible things, he does not have that by consensus. Rumi al-Haqa'iq: 2/253-254. 'He does not give' etc. contains an indication that it is not permissible for him to give, even if he takes him to the judge, the judge does not order him to give his share to him according to Abu Hanifa's saying, and that if he gives to him, it is not a division, so if the remainder perishes, the companion returns to the taker, and that one of them may take his share from it if he gets hold of it, and that if he gives and commits the forbidden, he is not liable. In Fatawa Qadi Khan, its meaning and wording: Three men deposited with a man, and they said: Do not give the money to any of us until we all gather, then he gave the share of one of them, Muhammad said: in qiyas he is liable, and Abu Hanifa said the same, and in istihsan he is not liable, and that is the saying of Abu Yusuf, and he made non-liability the istihsan, so it is the chosen view, as in al-Bahr [7:402]. Al-Maqdisi objected saying: How can it be the chosen view when all the matns are on the imam's view? Shaykh Qasim said: Al-Nasafi, al-Mahbubi, and Sadr al-Shari'a chose the imam's view. Al-Maqdisi said: Some say non-liability is the chosen view, arguing that istihsan contradicts what the imams established, and most matns agree on it, as in al-Hamawi. (Fath al-Mu'in) 'until the other comes' etc. according to Abu Hanifa, if he does so, he is liable for half, and according to the two, he gives his share and is not liable, and the dispute is concerning what is measured and weighed. In al-Dhakhira, the dispute is also mentioned concerning clothes and animals, and likewise in al-Kafi, and the correct view is that the dispute concerns fungibles like measured and weighed things, and regarding other things like clothes, animals, and slaves, the present one cannot take his share by consensus. (Mulla Miskin)
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn 'and if two men deposit something' (wa law awda‘ā shay’an). It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the depository (mūda‘) must deliver a share to one depositor before the other arrives. According to Imām Abū Hanīfa, the depository should not deliver until both are present; if he does deliver, he becomes liable for half of the value if the rest is lost. The two (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad al-Shaybānī) hold that he may deliver his share because the depositor is claiming his own property, just as if both were present. The dispute applies only to fungible goods (mā yukāl aw yūzan) such as grains and weights. For non-fungibles like clothes, animals, or slaves, there is consensus that the depository must not deliver a share alone. The commentary also discusses a scenario with three depositors who instruct the depository not to give until all gather. If he gives to one, there is difference: according to qiyas, he is liable (Abū Hanīfa and Muhammad), but according to istihsān, he is not (Abū Yūsuf). Some later scholars consider the istihsān view as the chosen one, but others, including al-Nasafī, prefer the imam's view. The final tarjīh is contested; the text does not decisively settle it.

Key Terms
المكيل والموزون (al-makīl wa al-mawzūn): Fungible goods that are sold by measure or weight, such as grains, oils, and precious metals.
ذوات الأمثال (dhawāt al-amthāl): Fungible goods that have exact substitutes and can be replaced by similar items.
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): Juristic preference; a method of avoiding rigidity in analogy by considering equity and custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the joint deposit scenario, the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn applies only to goods that are:
A) Fungible (dhawāt al-amthāl)
B) Non-fungible (qimiyyāt)
C) Both fungible and non-fungible
D) Only gold and silver

Key Takeaway
In a joint deposit of fungibles, Abu Hanifa forbids giving a share to one depositor alone, while the Sahibayn allow it without liability.

Liability for Handing Over a Divisible Deposit to a Co-Depository
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for handing over a divisible deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on whether a depository is liable when he hands over his portion of a divisible deposit to his co-depository, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
When a divisible deposit is shared, Abu Hanifa holds the giver liable; the Sahibayn do not.

This passage addresses a common scenario: two people jointly entrusted with a deposit. Since they cannot both keep the whole together for divisible items, they naturally divide. But what if one gives his share to the other? The Hanafi school has an internal divergence, rooted in how we interpret the owner's consent. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its practical ramifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the ruling concerns a depository giving his portion of a divisible deposit to the other depository. The question is: does this make him liable (daman)?
Divisible items: dirhams, dinars, etc.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds the giver liable because he failed to preserve the deposit by placing it in another's hand; the receiver is not liable as he is a sub-depository. The dalil is qiyas: the primary duty is preservation, and handing over without explicit consent is negligence.
The receiver is considered mūda‘ al-mūda‘ (depository of the depository).
3
State Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold neither liable because the owner, knowing they cannot both keep the whole together, implicitly consented to mutual division (muhāyā’a). The possession by one is thus authorized.
Their dalil is the owner's implied consent (iḍmār al-riḍā).
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the deposit is lost after being handed over, Abu Hanifa imposes liability on the giver, while Sahibayn exonerate both. The owner can claim compensation from the giver only under Abu Hanifa's view.
This affects the owner's recourse and the depositories' risk.
5
Distinguish divisible vs indivisible
For indivisible items (e.g., a large animal), there is consensus that no liability attaches when one gives full custody to the other, because the owner accepted each having independent control over the whole.
The text says: 'bikhilāf mā lā yuqsam' – unlike what cannot be divided.

Source Text

[رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٤/٢] اقتسماه: أي اقتسم الرجلان كالمكيل والموزون. بخلاف ما لا يقسم: فإنه لا يضمن بالدفع إلى الآخر؛ لأن المالك رضي بثبوت يد كل واحد منهما على الانفراد في الكل. رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٤/٢] ولو قال له إلخ : أي ولو قال المودع بالكسر للمودع بالفتح: لا تدفع الوديعة إلى عيالك أو قال له: احفظ الوديعة في هذا البيت وأشار إلى بيت فدفعها أي المودع إلى من لابد أي لا فراق له منه، أو حفظ الوديعة في بيت آخر من الدار التي تشتمل على البيت الذي عينه وعلى غيره، لم يضمن؛ أنه لا يمكنه الحفظ مع مراعاة هذا الشرط، فلم يكن مفيداً، فيلغو. هذا إذا كانت الوديعة مما يحفظ في يد من منعه المودع من الدفع إليه مثل أن يكون فرساً، فيمنعه من التسليم إلى غلامه أو يكون عقد جوهر، فيمنعه من التسليم إلى امرأته أو كانت بيوت الدار متساوية، وأما لو كانت الوديعة مما لا يحفظ في يد من نهاه عن الدفع إليه كما إذا كانت فرساً، فنهاه عن الدفع إلى امرأته، أو كانت عقد جوهر، فنهاه عن التسليم إلى ،غلامه أو كانت بيوت الدار مختلفة بأن كانت في بعضها عورة، فيضمن بالمخالفة؛ لأن التقييد في مثله مفيد رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٤/٢] لابد له منه من ذلك الشخص كدفع الدابة إلى عبده من الدار التي كلا البيتين في تلك الدار. (ملا مسكين) وإن كان له منه إلخ هذه المسألة صادقة بصورتين الأولى: أن تكون الوديعة شيئا خفيفا يمكن المودع بنفسه كالخاتم، فإنه يضمن بدفعه إلى عياله الثانية: أن يكون له عيال سوى من منعه من الدفع إليه. "بحر" فإن قلت: هذا إنما يتجه أن لو منعه من الدفع إلى بعض معين من عياله وهو خلاف ما يستفاد من قول المصنف: "ولو قال: تدفع إلى عيالك"، قلت: مبنى هذا الأشكال ما هو المتبادر من أن قوله: "وإن كان له منه بد" مرتبط بقوله: = لا الحفظ
And if a man deposits with two men something that can be divided, they divide it, and each keeps half, and if one gives to the other, he is liable, unlike what cannot be divided. And if he says to him: 'Do not give to your family,' or 'Keep it in this house,' and he gives it to someone he cannot avoid, or keeps it in another house of the building, he is not liable, if the keeper and the depositor are equal in preserving the deposit. And if he has an alternative to giving, then he is liable. And if a man deposits etc.: meaning if a man deposits with two men something divisible like dirhams and dinars, the two depositories divide it, and each keeps half, and if one gives what is in his hand to the other, he is liable according to Abu Hanifa, and the receiver is not liable because he is a depository of the depository. They two said: neither is liable, because when he deposited with them knowing that they cannot both keep it together in one place, he implied consent to mutual division. And with him (Abu Hanifa) it is because he neglected preservation by placing it in the hand of his companion. As for the consent mentioned, we say: what is expressly required is that they both preserve, and mutual division is a necessity which is avoided by dividing what is divisible and by alternating custody for what is not divisible, so handing over is not permissible. [Rumi al-Haqa'iq: 2/254] 'They divide it': i.e., the two men divide like measured and weighed items. 'Unlike what cannot be divided': then he is not liable by giving to the other, because the owner consented to each having independent control over the whole. [Rumi al-Haqa'iq: 2/254] 'And if he says to him' etc.: meaning if the depositor says to the depository: 'Do not give the deposit to your family,' or says to him: 'Keep the deposit in this house' pointing to a house, and he gives it to someone he cannot avoid, or keeps the deposit in another house of the same building that includes the specified house and others, he is not liable, because it is not possible to preserve while observing this condition, so it is futile and thus ignored. This is if the deposit is something that cannot be preserved in the hand of the one from whom he forbade giving to him, such as if it is a horse, he forbids him from handing it to his servant, or if it is a necklace of jewels, he forbids him from handing it to his wife, or if the houses of the building are equal. But if the deposit is something that can be preserved in the hand of the one he forbade giving to him, as if it is a horse and he forbids him from giving it to his wife, or if it is a necklace and he forbids giving it to his servant, or if the houses of the building are different such that some have defects, then he is liable for violating the condition, because the restriction is beneficial in such a case. [Rumi al-Haqa'iq: 2/254] 'He must give to that person' like giving the animal to his slave or to the house where both houses are in that building. (Mulla Miskin) 'And if he has an alternative' etc. This issue applies in two scenarios: first, if the deposit is light and the depository can carry it himself like a ring, then he is liable by giving it to his family; second, if he has other family besides the one he forbade him from giving to. [Bahr] If you say: This applies only if he forbade him from giving to a specific family member, which contradicts what is understood from the author's statement: 'and if he says: do not give to your family', I say: the basis of this difficulty is what is apparent that his statement 'and if he has an alternative' is connected to his statement = not preservation
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage covers two interconnected rulings. First, when a man deposits something divisible (e.g., coins) with two people, they must physically divide it, each keeping half. If one gives his half to the other, he is liable (according to Abu Hanifa) because he fails to preserve the deposit by handing it to his colleague. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that neither is liable, reasoning that the owner, by depositing with two, implicitly consented to mutual division (muhāyā’a) for items that cannot be kept together. For indivisible items (e.g., a large animal), the depository may give possession to the other without liability because the owner accepted each having full custody. Second, if the depositor gives specific instructions, e.g., 'do not give to your family' or 'keep in this house,' the depository's compliance is examined. If the instruction is impossible or futile (e.g., forbidding giving to a servant when the deposit is a horse that cannot be cared for without the servant), the condition is void and the depository is not liable if he gives to that person. If the instruction is practical and beneficial (e.g., forbidding giving to a wife for a horse, because a wife can handle it), then violating it makes the depository liable. The final part discusses when the depository has an alternative (e.g., he can personally carry a ring) and still gives it to family — then he is liable.

Key Terms
المهايأة (al-muhāyā’a): Mutual division or alternating custody of a shared property when simultaneous possession is impossible.
ما لا يقسم (mā lā yuqsam): Indivisible property that cannot be physically divided without loss of utility, e.g., a large animal or a building.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn regarding a depository who gives his half of a divisible deposit to the other?
A) Under Abu Hanifa, the giver is liable; under Sahibayn, neither is liable.
B) Under Abu Hanifa, the receiver is liable; under Sahibayn, both are liable.
C) Under both, the giver is liable, but the amount differs.
D) Under both, neither is liable if the item is divisible.

Key Takeaway
For divisible deposits, Abu Hanifa makes the giver liable while Sahibayn do not, but for indivisible deposits there is consensus on no liability.

Restrictive Instructions in Deposit: When Violation Leads to Liability
Ruling on when violating restrictive instructions in a deposit results in liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a depository's violation of the owner's restrictive instructions (e.g., not to give to family or not to keep elsewhere) results in liability, based on the futility or utility of the condition.

Core Idea
A depository is not liable for violating a condition that is impossible or futile; but is liable if the condition is practically beneficial.

Sometimes the owner of a deposit gives specific instructions: 'Don't give it to your family' or 'Keep it in this house.' This lesson covers when the depository can ignore such instructions without liability, and when he must comply or face liability. The key is whether the instruction is possible and meaningful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the owner's instruction and the depository's action
Read the passage: the owner says either 'Do not give to your family' or 'Keep in this house.' The depository gives to a person he cannot avoid (lā budda lahu minhu) or keeps in another house of the same building.
The instruction must be identified as a prohibition or a positive directive.
2
Check if the instruction is impossible or futile
If the depository cannot preserve the deposit without violating the instruction (e.g., a horse cannot be cared for without a servant, and the owner forbade giving to the servant), then the condition is futile (lā yufīd) and is ignored (yulghā). Thus, violating it incurs no liability.
The passage gives examples: a horse needs a servant; a necklace needs a wife (for women's care?) – careful: the text says horse to servant, necklace to wife is allowable? Actually, it says if the deposit is something that cannot be preserved in the hand of the forbidden person, like a horse forbidden to give to the servant (contradiction?), need to parse correctly.
3
Determine if the instruction is practically beneficial
If the deposit can be preserved without violating the instruction, and the instruction is meaningful (e.g., forbidding giving a horse to a wife because a wife can handle it, or forbidding storing in a defective room), then violating it makes the depository liable.
The passage contrasts: a horse given to a wife (she can manage) vs. a horse given to a servant (he is essential). This distinction turns on whether the forbidden person is necessary for preservation.
4
Apply the 'lā budda lahu minhu' principle
If the person to whom the deposit is given is essential for preservation (e.g., a servant for a horse), the prohibition is futile and void. The depository is not liable.
Also, if the house is changed to another house in the same building that is equally safe, the condition of a specific house is futile.
5
Consider when the depository has an alternative
The passage ends with: if the depository has an alternative (e.g., he can carry the light deposit himself) and still gives to family, he is liable. This applies when he has other family not forbidden, or can act personally.
This is the meaning of 'wa in kāna lahu minhu bad' – if he has an alternative to giving.

Joint Deposit with Two Depositees: Divisible Property
Ruling on joint deposit with two depositees and division of responsibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability when one joint depositee of a divisible deposit hands his portion to the other, and apply Abu Hanifa's ruling as the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
When a divisible deposit is jointly held, one depositee may not unilaterally transfer his share; doing so makes him liable.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute from the Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding joint deposits. When a divisible item like money is deposited with two trustees, the Hanafi school requires them to divide and guard separately. But what if one hands his portion to the other? The three early Hanafi masters differ. We will dissect the ruling, the reasoning, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the deposit: divisible property (e.g., dirhams) deposited with two depositees. One depositee gives his half to the other.
Divisible means the item can be split without loss of value.
2
Identify the Hanafi position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the hander-over is liable (damin) for loss; the recipient is not. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say neither is liable.
This is the core intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Extract the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: The hander-over abandoned his guarding duty by putting the item in his companion's hand. Sahibayn's proof: The owner knew they could not guard together, so he implicitly consented to any arrangement that facilitates custody.
Both proofs are aqli (rational), relying on the nature of the contract.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds that the explicit condition in a joint deposit is that both guard together. Necessity only permits division (for divisible items) or time-sharing (for indivisible), not unilateral transfer.
The distinction between divisible and indivisible is key.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, if the item is lost after transfer, the hander-over must compensate; under Sahibayn, neither does. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's view.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أودع رجل عند رجلين مما يقتسم اقتسماه، وحفظ كل نصفه، ولو دفع إلى كل واحد من الرجلين الآخر ضمن بخلاف ما لا يقسم. وإن أودع رجل إلخ: أي وإن أودع رجل عند رجلين مما يقسم كالدراهم والدنانير، اقتسمه المودعان، وحفظ كل واحد منهما نصفه، ولو دفع أحدهما ما في يده إلى الآخر، ضمن عند أبي حنيفة، ولا يضمن القابض؛ لأنه مودع المودع، وقالا: لا يضمنان؛ لأنه لما أودع عندهما مع علمه أنه لا يمكنهما الاجتماع على حفظهما في فقد مكان واحد، بالمهايأة والقسمة وله أنه ترك الحفظ بالوضع في يد صاحبه، وما ذكر من الرضا، قلنا: المصرح به حفظهما، وأما المهايأة والقسمة فضرورة وهي تندفع بالقسمة فيما يقسم، والمهايأة فيما لا يقسم، فلا يجوز الوضع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٤/٢] اقتسماه: أي اقتسم الرجلان كالمكيل والموزون. بخلاف ما لا يقسم: فإنه لا يضمن بالدفع إلى الآخر؛ لأن المالك رضي بثبوت يد كل واحد منهما على الانفراد في الكل. رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٤/٢]
If a man deposits with two men something that can be divided, they divide it and each keeps his half. And if one of them gives to the other what is in his hand, he is liable according to Abu Hanifa, and the recipient is not liable because he is a depositary of the depositary. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Neither is liable because when he deposited with both knowing that they cannot be together to guard it in one place, it is by arrangement and division. And his argument is that he abandoned guarding by placing it in the hand of his companion. As for the satisfaction mentioned, we say: the explicit condition is that both guard together, but arrangement and division are necessities, and they are removed by division for divisible items and by arrangement for indivisible items, so handing over is not allowed. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:254] 'They divide it' means the two men divide it like measured and weighed items. 'Unlike what cannot be divided' means he is not liable by giving it to the other because the owner consented to each having independent hand over the whole. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:254]
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses joint deposit (wadi'ah mushtarakah) where the deposited item is divisible, such as dirhams and dinars. The depositees must divide the item, and each guards his portion. If one depositee hands his portion to the other, he becomes liable (damin) according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while the recipient is not liable because he is considered a depositary of the depositary (mudi' al-mudi'). The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that neither is liable, reasoning that the owner knew they could not physically guard together, so he implicitly consented to time-sharing (muhaya'ah) or division. Abu Hanifa responds that the explicit stipulation is that both guard jointly; necessity allows division or arrangement, but one depositee cannot unilaterally transfer his responsibility to the other. This distinction between divisible and indivisible property is crucial: for indivisible items (e.g., a slave or cloth), the owner has consented to each having full hand, so no liability arises from one depositee handing control to the other.

Key Terms
المهايأة (al-muhaya'ah): A time-based or space-based division of custody between joint depositees, used when physical joint guarding is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if one of two joint depositees of divisible dirhams hands his portion to the other, what is the ruling?
A) Both are liable
B) Neither is liable
C) The hander-over is liable, the recipient is not
D) The recipient is liable, the hander-over is not

Key Takeaway
In a joint deposit with divisible property, a depositee who hands his share to the other becomes liable for loss, because the explicit condition of joint guarding is not fulfilled by unilateral transfer.

Liability When Moving a Deposit to Another House
Ruling on depositing in a completely different house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general ruling on liability when a depositee moves a deposit to another house, and identify the exception when the new house is similar or more secure.

Core Idea
Moving a deposit to another house makes the depositee liable unless the new house is equally or more secure.

This lesson addresses a common issue in deposits (wadi'ah): what happens when the depositee moves the deposit to a different location. The owner's trust is based on the original location; changing it may be a breach unless the new location is at least as secure. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling with an exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The depositee is liable (ضمن) if he moves the deposit to another house.
This is the default ruling.
2
Identify the exception
He is not liable if the other house is similar (مثلها) or more secure (أحرز منها).
The exception is derived from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
3
Understand the rationale
People differ in trustworthiness and knowledge of preservation; the depositee's judgment is trusted only in the original context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو حفظها في دار أخرى ضمن. هذا محمول على ما إذا لم تكن الدار الأخرى مثلها، أما لو كانت مثلها أو أحرز منها لا يضمن كذا في البحر الرائق: ٤٠٢/٧] عن "الخلاصة". (فتح) ضمن إلخ أي ضمن المودع في كلا الصورتين؛ لأن الناس يختلفون في الأمانة والكياسة ومعرفة طرق الصيانة رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٤/٢]
Or if he kept it in another house, he is liable. This applies when the other house is not similar; if it is similar or more secure, he is not liable, as in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:402 from al-Khulasa. (Fath) 'He is liable' means the depositee is liable in both cases because people differ in trustworthiness, intelligence, and knowledge of ways of preservation. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:254]
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is a brief ruling on depositing the item in a completely separate house (not within the same compound). The general rule is that moving the deposit to a different house makes the depositee liable. However, an exception is noted: if the alternative house is equal or superior in security, then no liability arises. The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies this nuance. The reason for liability is that the owner trusts the depositee's judgment specifically in the original context; moving to another house changes that context.

Key Terms
ضمن (daman): Liable, i.e., the depositee must compensate for loss or damage.
مثلها (mithluha): Similar to the original house in terms of security and preservation.
أحرز منها (ahraz minha): More secure than the original house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a depositee moves the deposit to a house that is more secure than the one specified by the owner, is he liable?
A) Yes, because he changed location
B) No, because the new house is more secure
C) Yes, unless the owner explicitly allowed it
D) No, only if the new house is less secure

Key Takeaway
The depositee is liable if he moves the deposit to another house unless the alternative is equally or more secure.

Depositary of a Usurper (Ghasib)
Ruling on deposit made with a usurper and owner's options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the liability of a depositary of a usurper (ghasib) and explain the owner's option to claim from either party, as well as the depositary's right of recourse against the usurper.

Core Idea
The depositary of a usurper is a guarantor because possession without owner's consent is invalid.

This passage addresses the fiqh of trust (amanah) when it is broken by unlawful possession. When a usurper deposits stolen property with a third party, that third party becomes a guarantor, and the owner has the right to claim from either. A key discussion among Hanafi scholars is whether the depositary can then recover from the usurper if he knew of the usurpation. We will explore the ruling, the owner's options, and the relied-upon position on recourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and primary ruling
The passage concerns the depositary of a usurper (mudi' al-ghasib). The primary ruling is that such a depositary is liable (damin) because he took possession without the owner's permission.
This liability is independent of the depositary's knowledge of the usurpation.
2
Describe the owner's option
The owner may choose to claim compensation from either the usurper (ghasib) or the depositary. This is a practical benefit to the owner.
The owner is not limited to suing only the usurper.
3
Analyze the depositary's right of recourse
After being held liable, the depositary may seek recourse from the usurper. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding this right when the depositary knew the depositor was a usurper.
The majority view (zahir al-riwayah) allows recourse even with knowledge.
4
Identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The later authorities (al-Durr, al-Fath) prefer the majority view that the depositary can recourse regardless of knowledge. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
The minority view (Abu al-Yusr) is not relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومودع الغاصب ضامن لا مودع المودع معه. ومودع الغاصب حتى لو غصب رجل شيئاً، فأودع عند رجل، فهلك عنده ضمن، والمالك مخير إن شاء ضمن المودع، وإن شاء ضمن الغاصب، وذكر أبو اليسر الله إن لم يعلم أن المودع غاصب يرجع عليه بعد التضمين، وإن علم لا يرجع وكذا أشار إليه السرخسي عليه كذا في "ملا" "مسكين، وقال في "الفتح": استظهر في "الدر" أنه يرجع وإن علم كما في "الدرر" خلافاً للقهستاني والباقلاني والبرجندي وغيرهم لكونه عاملاً للغاصب. قال العيني عند قول المصنف: مودع الغصب ضامن"؛ لأنه قبض المال بغير إذن المالك، ثم مودع الغاصب إن لم يعلم أن المودع غاصب رجع على الغاصب قولاً واحداً، وإن علم فكذلك في ظاهر الرواية، وحكى أبو اليسر أنه لا يرجع. رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٤/٢]
The depositary of a usurper is liable, not the depositary of a depositary with him. The depositary of a usurper: if a person usurps something and deposits it with another, and it perishes in his possession, he is liable. The owner has the option: if he wishes, he can hold the depositary liable, and if he wishes, he can hold the usurper liable. Abu al-Yusr mentioned that if the depositary did not know the depositor was a usurper, he can recourse against him after being held liable; if he knew, he cannot. Similarly, al-Sarakhsi indicated this. In "Fath", it is stated that in "al-Durr" it is preferred that he can recourse even if he knew, as in "al-Durar", contrary to al-Qahistani, al-Baqallani, al-Barjandi, etc., because he is acting for the usurper. Al-'Ayni said regarding the author's statement: 'The depositary of a usurper is liable' because he took possession without the owner's permission. Then, if the depositary did not know the depositor was a usurper, he can recourse against the usurper by unanimous opinion; if he knew, the same is the apparent narration, but Abu al-Yusr reported that he cannot. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2:254]
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where a usurper (ghasib) deposits the usurped item with a third party (mudi' al-ghasib). The third party is considered a guarantor (damin) because he took possession without the owner's consent. The owner can choose to claim compensation from either the usurper or the depositary. A key question is whether the depositary can then recourse against the usurper. The majority view (zahir al-riwayah) holds that he can recourse regardless of whether he knew the depositor was a usurper. A minority view, attributed to Abu al-Yusr, says he cannot recourse if he knew. The Hanafi school follows the majority view, as stated in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and other relied-upon works.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghasib): One who takes property without owner's permission, i.e., a usurper.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person unknowingly accepts a deposit from a usurper, and the deposit is lost, can the owner sue the depositary?
A) No, only the usurper can be sued
B) Yes, the owner can choose to sue the depositary
C) Yes, but only if the depositary knew of the usurpation
D) No, the depositary is not liable if he acted in good faith

Key Takeaway
The depositary of a usurper is liable to the owner, and the owner may choose to claim from either party; the depositary can then recourse against the usurper even if he knew of the usurpation.

The Depositary of a Depositary: Who Bears the Loss?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for re-depositing a trust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on liability when a depositary re-deposits without permission and apply the distinction between mudi' al-mudi' and mudi' al-ghasib.

Core Idea
Only the first depositary is liable; the second is a depositary of the depositary, not a usurper.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about who is liable when a depositary (first) re-deposits the trust item with a third party without the owner's permission. The key question: does the second depositary become directly liable to the owner? Abu Hanifa and the two imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on this point. Understanding their reasoning clarifies the broader principles of liability in deposits and usurpation.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A owner deposits an item with a first depositary. That depositary, without the owner's permission, deposits it with a second person (not his family). The item is then lost or destroyed.
The second depositary is called 'mudi' al-mudi'' (depositary of the depositary).
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules that only the first depositary is liable (daman). The second depositary is not liable to the owner.
The first depositary breached his duty by re-depositing without permission.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the owner can choose to hold either depositary liable. If the owner holds the second liable, the second can recourse against the first.
This gives the owner more flexibility.
4
Understand the rationale – distinction from mudi' al-ghasib
The text contrasts with the case of a depositary of a usurper (mudi' al-ghasib). In that case, the second is also a usurper because the first's possession was unlawful from the start. Here, the first depositary initially had lawful possession; his liability only arises upon breach (after separation). The second's possession is derived from the first, not from a usurpation.
This distinction is the basis of Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Apply the rule
Given a similar scenario: if the owner sues the second depositary under Abu Hanifa's view, the second would not be liable. Under the Sahibayn, the owner could recover from the second, and the second would then sue the first.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whom the owner can sue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مودع المودع: أي لا يضمن مودع المودع بأن أودع عند رجل وديعة، فأودعها المودع عند شخص آخر من غير عياله، فهلك، ضمن الأول دون الثاني عند أبي حنيفة له وعندهما له أن يضمن أيهما شاء، فإن ضمن الأول لم يرجع على الثاني، وإن ضمن الثاني رجع على الأول، كذا في "ملا" مسكين"، قال في "البحر الرائق": والفرق بينهما أي مودع الغاصب ومودع المودع على قول أبي حنيفة: أن مودع الغاصب غاصب؛ لعدم إذن المالك ابتداء وبقاء، وفي الثاني ليس بغاصب؛ لأنه لا يضمن المودع بمجرد الدفع ما لم يفارقه، وإذا ضمن مودع الغاصب رجع على الغاصب مطلقاً علم أنه غاصب أولا، وإذا ضمن مودع الغاصب ضمن غاصب الغاصب، والمشتري منه بالأولى، وقد تقدم في المضاربة أنّ المضارب لو دفع المال مضاربة بلا إذن لا يضمن واحد منهما قبل عمل الثاني. [البحر الرائق: ٤٠٣/٧]
The depositary of a depositary: i.e., the depositary of a depositary is not liable. If someone deposited an item with a man, and that depositary deposited it with another person who is not his family, and it perishes, the first is liable but not the second according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the owner can hold either of them liable: if he holds the first liable, the first cannot recourse against the second; if he holds the second liable, the second can recourse against the first. This is in Mulla Miskin. In al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the difference between the depositary of a usurper and the depositary of a depositary according to Abu Hanifa is that the depositary of a usurper is a usurper because the owner's permission is absent from the beginning and continuing; while in the second case, he is not a usurper because the depositary does not become liable merely by handing over until he separates. If the depositary of a usurper is held liable, he can recourse against the usurper absolutely, whether he knew he was a usurper or not; and if the depositary of a usurper is held liable, then the depositary of the usurper's depositary, and the buyer from him, are also liable. It has been presented in Mudarabah that if the mudarib hands over the capital without permission, neither is liable before the second works with it. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:403]
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two cases: (1) depositary of a usurper (mudi' al-ghasib) and (2) depositary of a depositary (mudi' al-mudi'). In the second case, the original depositary re-deposits the item with a third party without the owner's permission. According to Abu Hanifa, only the first depositary is liable, and the second is not. The two imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the owner can choose to hold either liable, but if he holds the second, the second can recourse against the first. The difference is that the usurper's possession is unlawful from the start, so any subsequent depositary is also a usurper. In contrast, the first depositary initially had lawful possession; his liability only arises when he breaches the deposit (e.g., by handing over to a non-family member). The second depositary is not liable because he is a depositary of the first, not of the owner.

Key Terms
مُودِع (mudi'): depositor (one who gives something in trust)
وَدِيعَة (wadi'ah): deposit; trust item
غَاصِب (ghasib): usurper; one who takes property unlawfully
ضَمَان (daman): liability (to compensate for loss)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a depositary deposits the item with a third party without permission and it is lost, who is liable?
A) Only the third party
B) Both the first depositary and the third party
C) Only the first depositary
D) Neither, because it was lost accidentally

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mufta bihi position is that only the original depositary is liable when he re-deposits without permission; the second depositary is not liable to the owner.

Two Claimants of a Deposit: Ruling on Oaths
Ruling on a deposit dispute with two claimants and the depositary's refusal to swear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a depositary refuses to take an oath in a two-claimant deposit dispute and identify the consequences of such refusal.

Core Idea
Refusal to swear by the depositary makes him liable to both claimants.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a specific scenario in deposit (wadi‘ah): two men each claim ownership of a sum of money held by a third party. When the depositary refuses to swear an oath denying their claims, the Hanafi school imposes a severe consequence—the deposit becomes joint property of the claimants, and the depositary must pay an equivalent amount as compensation. This ruling illustrates the principle that nukul (refusal to swear) can operate as a quasi‑confession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the deposit
A man (the depositary) holds 1000 dirhams. Two men each claim that this money belongs to him and was deposited by him. Identify the core elements: the depositary (mūdi‘), the claimants (mudda‘iyān), and the deposited property (wadī‘ah).
2
Determine the depositary's action after demand
The claimants demand an oath from the depositary. He refuses (nakala) to swear to either of them. This refusal is the key trigger for the ruling.
Nukul: refusal to take an oath after being required to do so.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on nukul
In Hanafi fiqh, nukul is treated as an admission of liability when the party is required to swear and refuses without valid reason. Therefore, the depositary's refusal is equivalent to confessing that both claims are valid.
This is similar to the rule that nukul in a debt claim leads to liability.
4
Derive the consequences
Since both claims are deemed true, the 1000 dirhams become joint property of the two claimants. Moreover, because the depositary was entrusted with the deposit of each, he must pay an additional 1000 dirhams to be divided equally between them.
Total liability: 2000 dirhams (1000 original + 1000 compensation).
5
Consider the alternative scenarios
The text notes that if the depositary swears to both, nothing is owed. If he swears to only one, the one who did not receive an oath may still have a claim; the standard Hanafi ruling is that the one sworn to loses his claim, and the other gets the deposit.
The passage is incomplete here, but the principle is that swearing to one claimant discharges the depositary from that claim, but he remains liable to the other unless he also swears to him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معه ألف إلخ أي مع رجل ألف درهم ادعى رجلان كل واحد منهما ادعى أنه أي الألف له، أودعه أي الألف إياه أي الرجل، فنكل أي الرجل عن الحلف لهما أي الرجلين بعد أن استحلفا فالألف الذي مع الرجل يكون لهما أي الرجلين وعليه أي على الرجل الذي معه الألف ألف آخر يكون بينهما أي بين الرجلين؛ لأن دعواهما صحيحة، فيجب عليه اليمين لهما، فإن حلف لهما، فلا شيء لهما عليه لعدم الحجة، وإن حلف لأحدهما، =
With him is a thousand [dirhams] etc.: i.e., a man has a thousand dirhams. Two men each claim that the thousand belongs to him and that he deposited it with the man. Then the man refuses to swear to both after they demanded an oath. Then the thousand that is with the man belongs to both of them, and upon the man who has the thousand is another thousand to be divided between the two men, because their claim is valid. So it is obligatory for him to swear to both. If he swears to both, nothing is due to them due to lack of proof. If he swears to one, ...
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a case in deposit (wadi'ah) where two men each claim that a specific sum of money (1000 dirhams) held by a third man was deposited by them individually. The holder refuses to take an oath. The ruling is that the 1000 dirhams become jointly owned by the two claimants, and additionally, the holder must pay another 1000 dirhams to be divided between them. This is because their claims are deemed valid in the absence of an oath denying them. If the holder does swear to both claimants, then nothing is owed. If he swears to only one, the implication (though the text cuts off) is that the one who was sworn to loses his claim, while the other might still have a claim? The standard Hanafi ruling is that if he swears to one, the other gets the deposit. Here the text is incomplete, but the principle is clear: the depositary must swear to each claimant; if he refuses, he must compensate both.

Key Terms
نكل (nakala): Refused to take an oath after being required to do so.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where two men claim a deposit of 1000 dirhams and the holder refuses to swear to either, what is the total amount the holder must ultimately pay?
A) 1000 dirhams (the original deposit)
B) 2000 dirhams (original plus compensation)
C) 0 dirhams, because refusal is not admission
D) 1000 dirhams to each claimant separately (total 2000, but no joint ownership)

Key Takeaway
When a depositary refuses to swear an oath denying multiple claims on the same deposit, Hanafi law treats the refusal as an admission, making him liable for the deposit plus an equivalent compensation.

Liability in Deposit: Changing Houses, Usurper's Deposit, and Sub-deposit
Explains rulings on depositing in another house and depositary of a usurper

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the liability of a depositary who moves the deposit, the depositary of a usurper, and the depositary of a depositary, and distinguish the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on sub-deposit.

Core Idea
A depositary becomes liable when he exceeds the limits of the deposit contract; the treatment of sub-deposit differs from the usurper's deposit.

This lesson opens Kitab al-Wadi'ah with three key scenarios that test the boundaries of trust and liability. You will learn when a depositary is held liable for loss, the special case of a usurper's deposit, and the disputed sub-deposit case between Abu Hanifa and his students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and locate the three distinct rulings: (a) depositary moves deposit to another house, (b) a usurper deposits stolen goods with a person, (c) a depositary sub-deposits with a third party.
The passage begins with 'أو حفظها في دار أخرى' then discusses 'مودع الغاصب' and 'مودع المودع'.
2
State the ruling and reasoning for each
For each scenario, determine whether the depositary is liable and why. The key concept is ta'addi (exceeding limits). For moving house, liability depends on whether the new house is equally safe. For usurper's deposit, the depositary is liable because possession is without owner's consent. For sub-deposit, according to Abu Hanifa, the first depositary is liable but the second is not.
The passage includes commentary that the liability for moving to another house is mitigated if the new house is equal or more secure.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on sub-deposit
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ from Abu Hanifa: they allow the owner to choose to sue either the first or the second depositary. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
The matn follows Abu Hanifa's view, indicating it is the relied-upon position.

Commentary
This passage opens Kitab al-Wadi'ah with several rulings. First, if the depositary keeps the deposit in a different house without permission, he becomes liable for its loss unless the new house is equal or safer. Second, if a usurper deposits stolen goods with someone, that depositary is liable to the owner, who may choose to sue either the usurper or the depositary. There is a dispute regarding whether the depositary can recover from the usurper if he knew he was a usurper. Third, the depositary of a depositary (sub-deposit) is not liable according to Abu Hanifa; only the first depositary is liable. The Sahibayn allow the owner to choose whom to sue. The passage then explains the difference between mudi' al-ghasib and mudi' al-mudi'.

مُودَع المُودَع (mudi' al-mudi'): The person to whom the original depositary sub-deposits the item; he is not liable according to Abu Hanifa.
تَعَدٍّ (ta'addī): Exceeding the limits of permission, which triggers liability.

Key Takeaway
The depositary is liable for exceeding contract limits; the usurper's depositary is liable; the sub-depositary is not liable per Abu Hanifa, but the owner may have a choice per Sahibayn.

6 min
Two Claimants to a Deposit: Ruling on Nukul (Refusal to Swear)
Explains the ruling when two persons claim a deposit and the depositary refuses to swear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a depositary refuses to swear to two claimants and explain the legal consequence of nukul as a form of admission.

Core Idea
Refusal to swear (nukul) is treated as admission, making the depositary liable for the deposit and an equivalent amount.

This passage addresses a classic dispute in deposit law: two persons each claim that a man is holding a thousand dirhams as a deposit from him. The depositary has no evidence. He is required to swear an oath to each claimant. What happens if he refuses? The Hanafi school treats his refusal as an admission, leading to a surprising but logical liability. Understanding nukul is essential for grasping how Islamic courts handle evidentiary stalemates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the setting: a depositary (mudiʿ) has 1000 dirhams. Two claimants each say it is their deposit. No evidence exists. The depositary is asked to swear he owes nothing to either.
2
Classify the Defendant's Action
The depositary's refusal (nukul) is not mere silence. In Hanafi fiqh, nukul in response to a claim is equivalent to an admission (iqrar) of the claim.
This is a key principle: nukul functions as proof against the defendant.
3
Apply the Ruling to Nukul for Both
If he refuses to swear to both claimants, his nukul is taken as admitting both claims. The 1000 dirhams are split between them (500 each). Moreover, since each had claimed the entire amount, his admission makes him liable for a full 1000 to each. To avoid unjust enrichment, he must pay another 1000 dirhams from his own wealth, which is also split between them.
This ensures each claimant gets his claimed amount while the depositary bears the cost of his refusal.
4
Apply the Ruling to Partial Nukul
If he swears to one claimant but refuses to the other, the one he swore to loses his claim (due to lack of proof), while the other claimant gets the entire deposit based on the nukul.
The oath clears him from one claim; nukul binds him to the other.
5
Understand the Judicial Procedure
The judge may administer the oath to either claimant first. To avoid bias and maintain fairness, drawing lots (qurʿah) is recommended when there is disagreement.
This procedural detail shows the Hanafi concern for equity in adjudication.

Commentary
This passage presents a case in deposit law: A man has 1000 dirhams. Two men each claim it as a deposit. The depositary has no evidence. He is required to swear to both claimants. The passage also discusses that the judge may start with either claimant, and it is recommended to draw lots to avoid bias.

'Umra and Sukna: Definition and General Rule of Revocability
Defines 'umra and sukna and states the lender's right to revoke at any time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'umra and sukna as types of 'ariyah and state the Hanafi ruling that the lender may revoke the loan at any time, with evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
A life grant of dwelling is a revocable loan, not a gift of ownership — the lender may take it back whenever he wishes.

This lesson introduces two specific types of loans in Hanafi law: 'umra (life grant) and sukna (dwelling). The core ruling is that any loan ('ariyah) is inherently non-binding, meaning the lender can reclaim the item at any time, even if a duration was agreed. We will examine the linguistic definitions and the Prophetic proof that underpins this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and their legal category
Locate 'umra and sukna in the text. Note that they are subtypes of 'ariyah (loan). Understand that 'umra refers to granting dwelling for the recipient's lifetime. The commentary defines 'umra as an absolute object: 'I made it a life grant for you'.
The term 'umra is derived from i'mar, meaning to grant for life.
2
State the general Hanafi rule
From the text: 'ويرجع المعير متى شاء' — the lender may revoke whenever he wishes. This establishes that the loan contract is non-binding (ghayr lazim).
This is the default ruling for all loans in the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the Prophetic proof
The hadith 'al-minhatu maruddatun wa al-ariyatu mu'adatun' is cited. Explain how each part indicates returnability and revocability.
Note that 'maniha' is a specific type of loan, but the ruling generalizes.
4
Apply the rule to timed loans
The commentator explicitly extends the rule to loans with a specified time. Even if a duration was agreed, the lender may revoke, as the contract remains non-binding.
This can cause harm; the remedy is discussed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أحكام العارية 
عمري سكنى"، ويرجع المعير متى شاء،
متى أي مدة عمرك
لعدم لزومها أي العارية
أي المستعير
أي العارية
عمرى سكنى إلخ والعمرى اسم من الإعمار معناه: جعلت سكناها لك مدة عمرك فعمرى مفعول مطلق لفعل محذوف تقديره : أعمرتها لك عمرا وسكنى تمييز عن النسبة إلى المخاطب وهذا أولى مما في "المغرب" من أنه حال، نعم يجوز أن يكون خبراً و"لك" متعلق به أو بالنسبة بين المبتدأ والخبر كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الدِّينَ عِنْدَ الله الإسلام (آل عمران: ۱۹) حموي عن "الحفيد" على "صدر الشريعة". (فتح) ويرجع المعير إلخ لقوله عليه: "المنحة مردودة، والعارية مؤداة، ووجه الاستدلال ظاهر، وفيه تعميم بعد التخصيص؛ لما عرف أن المنحة عارية خاصة. (عناية) ولأن المنافع تحدث شيئا فشيئا، ويثبت الملك فيها بحسب حدوثها، فرجوعه امتناع عن تمليك ما لم يحدث، وله ذلك. تبيين الحقائق: ٣٤/٦] وأطلق في قوله: "ويرجع المعير شاء"، فشمل ما لو كانت موقتة، وفيه ضرر بين بالمستعير فإن الإعارة تبطل وتبقى العين بأجر المثل كمن استعار أمة لترضع ولده، وصار لا يأخذ إلا ثديها، فلها أجر المثل إلى الفطام، "بحر" عن "الخانية"، وفي "الدر" عن "الأشباه : العارية تلزم فيما إذا استعار جدار غيره لوضع جذوعه، فوضعها ثم باع المعير الجدار، ليس للمشتري رفعها، وقيل: نعم، إلا إذا شرطه وقت البيع، قلت: وبالقيل جزم في "الخلاصة" و"البزازية" وغيرهما إلخ. (فتح)
Book of al-'Ariyah. Rulings of al-'Ariyah. 'Umra (life grant) and sukna (dwelling): the lender may revoke whenever he wishes. ... 'Umra sukna etc. Al-'Umra is a noun from i'mar, meaning: I made it a dwelling for you for the duration of your life. 'Umra is an absolute object for an omitted verb: 'I granted it to you as a life grant.' Sukna is a specification after the noun. This is preferable to what is in al-Mughrib that it is a state. Yes, it may be a predicate, and 'for you' is attached to it or to the relation between subject and predicate, as in the verse 'Indeed the religion with Allah is Islam.' (Himawi from al-Hafid on Sadr al-Shari'ah). (Fath) 'And the lender may revoke' etc. because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'The gift (maniha) is returnable, and the loan (ariyah) is to be repaid.' The face of deduction is apparent. In this there is generalization after specification because it is known that maniha is a specific type of ariyah. (Inayah) And because benefits (manafi') arise little by little, and ownership is established in them according to their occurrence. So his revocation is a refusal to grant ownership of what has not yet arisen, and he has that right. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 34/6. And he said generally in his statement 'and the lender may revoke whenever he wishes' inclusive of when it is timed, and in that there is clear harm to the borrower, for the loan becomes void and the item remains subject to hire at market rate, like one who borrows a slave woman to nurse his child, and the child takes only her breast, so she is entitled to hire at market rate until weaning. (Bahr from al-Khaniyyah) And in al-Durr from al-Ashbah: The loan becomes binding when someone borrows the wall of another to place his beams, then places them, then the lender sells the wall; the buyer does not have the right to remove them. And it is said: Yes, except if he stipulates [the right] at the time of sale. I say: The latter view is stated definitively in al-Khulasa, al-Bazzaziyyah, and others. (Fath)
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It defines 'umra (life grant) and sukna (dwelling) as types of 'ariyah (loan). The key ruling is that the lender (mu'ir) may revoke the loan at any time, even if a specific duration was mentioned. The commentator explains the linguistic meaning: 'umra is an absolute object meaning 'I made it a life grant for you'. The proof is the hadith 'al-minhatu maruddatun wa al-ariyatu mu'adatun' (the gift is returnable and the loan is to be repaid). The deduction is clear: since a loan is essentially a temporary grant of use, the lender retains ownership and can reclaim the item. The commentary then addresses the problem of timed loans: if the lender revokes a timed loan, the borrower may suffer harm. The Hanafi solution is that the loan becomes void, but the borrower may be compensated for the remaining period through ujrat al-mithl (market hire). An example is given of a borrowed slave woman for nursing: if the lender revokes, the borrower must pay hire until weaning. However, there is a case where a loan becomes binding: if someone borrows a wall to place beams, and then the wall is sold, the buyer cannot remove the beams. This is an exception due to the need for stability. The passage thus establishes the general rule of revocability and its exceptions, all within Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
عمرى ('umra): A grant of dwelling for the lifetime of the recipient.
عارية (ariyah): A contract granting free use of a non-consumable item, revocable at the lender's will.
معير (mu'ir): The lender who gives the borrowed item.
مستعير (musta'ir): The borrower who receives the loan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, at any time, even if timed.
B) No, the loan is binding for the agreed period.
C) Only if the borrower did not start using the item.
D) Only if both parties agree to revocation.

Key Takeaway
A loan in Hanafi law is non-binding; the lender may revoke at any time, even if a duration was agreed, subject to compensation for the borrower's harm.
